2:23:1242:0:14:72:528:700:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Where Does Voucher Funding Go? How Large-Scale Subsidy Programs Affect Private-School
2:24:1327:14:14:72:230:686:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Revenue, Enrollment, and Prices
2:25:1358:14:14:72:259:672:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Daniel M. Hungerman and Kevin Rinz
2:26:1392:14:14:72:234:658:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:NBER Working Paper No. 21687
2:27:1420:14:14:72:138:644:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:October 2015
2:28:1432:14:28:72:162:630:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:JEL No. H2,I2,I22
2:29:1449:28:28:273:338:602:12:Times:12:Times:C:ABSTRACT
2:30:1457:28:14:72:540:574:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Using a new dataset constructed from nonprofit tax-returns, this paper explores how vouchers and
2:31:1553:14:14:72:540:560:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:other large-scale programs subsidizing private school attendance have affected the fiscal outcomes
2:32:1651:14:14:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:of private schools and the affordability of a private education. We find that subsidy programs created
2:33:1753:14:14:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:a large transfer of public funding to private schools, suggesting that every dollar of funding increased
2:34:1857:14:14:72:540:518:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:revenue by a dollar or more. Turning to the incidence of subsidies and the impact of subsidies on
2:35:1954:14:14:72:540:504:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:enrollment, our findings depend on the type of program introduced, with programs restricting eligibility
2:36:2058:14:14:72:540:490:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:to certain groups of students creating relatively large enrollment gains and small price increases compared
2:37:2165:14:14:72:540:476:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:to unrestricted programs. We calculate elasticities of demand and supply for private schools, and discuss
2:38:2270:14:42:72:146:462:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:welfare effects.
2:39:2286:42:14:72:180:420:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Daniel M. Hungerman
2:40:2305:14:14:72:198:406:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Department of Economics
2:41:2328:14:14:72:198:392:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:University of Notre Dame
2:42:2352:14:14:72:153:378:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:439 Flanner Hall
2:43:2368:14:14:72:211:364:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Notre Dame, IN 46556-5602
2:44:2393:14:14:72:124:350:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:and NBER
2:45:2401:14:28:72:164:336:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:dhungerm@nd.edu
2:46:2416:28:14:72:127:308:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Kevin Rinz
2:47:2426:14:14:72:198:294:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:University of Notre Dame
2:48:2450:14:14:72:153:280:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:434 Flanner Hall
2:49:2466:14:14:72:183:266:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Notre Dame, IN 46556

3:51:2498:0:30:72:161:710:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:Introduction
3:52:2510:30:20:72:540:680:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The United States is currently undergoing a revolution in the subsidization of private education, with dozens
3:53:2619:20:20:72:540:660:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of states considering or recently enacting large-scale laws that use public resources to promote attendance
3:54:2726:20:20:72:540:640:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:at private elementary and secondary schools.  Currently, over a million U.S. families participate in such
3:55:2831:20:20:72:540:620:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:programs and recent legislation in several states will push this number higher in coming years (Friedman
3:56:2935:20:20:72:154:600:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Foundation, 2013).
3:57:2953:20:20:86:539:580:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:But the proliferation of these programs across the country should not be taken as evidence that observers
3:58:3058:20:20:72:540:560:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:agree on how they work. Several elements of subsidy programs remain highly controversial. In particular,
3:59:3162:20:20:72:540:540:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:arguments often focus on thefinancialimplications of these policies.  On the one hand, these programs
3:60:3263:20:20:72:540:520:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are sometimes depicted as providing resources to disadvantaged families (Governor Mitch Daniels, 2011;
3:61:3365:20:20:72:540:500:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Governor Jan Brewer, 2012) or to wealthy families whose children would attend private school in any event
3:62:3470:20:19:72:540:480:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(Reese, 2009; People for the American Way, 2003). But families are not the only potential beneficiaries of
3:63:3576:19:20:72:540:461:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:private-school subsidies; some observers emphasize that these subsidies represent a new and unprecedented
3:64:3681:20:20:72:540:441:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:source of financial support for schools themselves (Crothers, 2014). The validity of these descriptions depends
3:65:3792:20:20:72:540:421:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:upon how these programs impact the cost and prevalence of private education, and whether subsidy funding
3:66:3896:20:20:72:236:401:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:ultimately goes to families or schools.
3:67:3935:20:20:86:539:381:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Prior research offers little evidence regarding these outcomes.  Of course, there is a large literature
3:68:4038:20:20:72:540:361:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:exploring the impact of voucher programs on economically important activities; Figlio (2009), Hoxby (2003),
3:69:4145:20:20:72:540:341:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and the Center on Education Policy (2011) provide useful background on research here. But most of this prior
3:70:4253:20:20:72:540:321:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:work considers outcomes such as student achievement, Tiebout sorting, and public-school performance. Using
3:71:4359:20:20:72:540:301:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:sophisticated theoretical models, some studies predict how vouchers might impact private school enrollment
3:72:4465:20:20:72:540:281:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:or tuition (Epple and Romano, 1998; Ferreyra, 2007; and Nechyba, 2000) but the empirical validity of these
3:73:4571:20:20:72:540:261:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:studies is unproven. Evidence for how subsidies affect private school finances, or evidence on the incidence
3:74:4679:20:20:72:514:241:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of school subsidies generally, is difficult to find. The goal of this paper is to contribute such evidence.
3:75:4786:20:20:86:539:221:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:A key challenge in doing so is finding data on private-school finances. For this, we turn to a largely-
3:76:4889:20:19:72:540:201:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:unnoticed source of data: nonprofit tax returns. Using a dataset of IRS Form 990 returns made available by
3:77:4995:19:20:72:540:182:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the National Center for Charitable Statistics, we construct a panel dataset with detailed financial informa-
3:78:5103:20:20:72:540:162:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:tion for thousands of private schools each year. Our sample covers about 20% of all private school enrollment
3:79:5212:20:20:72:540:142:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in the country. While some important groups of private schools, such as Catholic schools, are not included
3:80:5318:20:20:72:540:122:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in the sample, we provide several pieces of evidence which give us optimism that estimates from this sample
3:81:5425:20:20:72:261:102:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:could be informative for all private schools.
3:82:5470:20:0:86:539:82:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:We first investigate whether school revenue increases when a policy to subsidize private schooling is
4:83:5572:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:implemented. We focus on large-scale statewide subsidy programs. These programs are notable for their
4:84:5673:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:extraordinary (and ongoing) proliferation, and their large size suggests that they could have important effects
4:85:5784:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:on school enrollment and finances even at the state level, unlike the smaller-scale voucher programs that
4:86:5889:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:have been the focus of past work.  Our data cover the years 1991 to 2009; there are several states that
4:87:5992:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:enacted large-scale programs to subsidize private-school attendance that we use in our baseline estimates.
4:88:6098:20:20:72:375:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:We discuss these programs and their enactment in more detail below.
4:89:6165:20:20:86:539:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:We find robust evidence that school subsidies raise revenue for the private schools in our sample and that
4:90:6271:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the magnitude of this effect is large; even conservative extrapolations to all private schools indicate that a
4:91:6381:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:dollar of subsidy funding raises private school revenue by a dollar or more. However, we find that the impact
4:92:6490:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:depends crucially on the type of program enacted. Several programs we study place important restrictions
4:93:6594:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:on which students in a state can qualify for the subsidy (e.g., the programs are means-tested). We call these
4:94:6703:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:restricted programs. In contrast, unrestricted programs explicitly or in practice allow families to receive
4:95:6810:20:20:72:504:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsidies for expenditures on their own children and are not means tested nor otherwise restricted.
4:96:6909:20:19:86:539:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:We find evidence of private school enrollment growth following the establishment of a restricted program,
4:97:7014:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:but not for unrestricted programs. We further find increases in per-student revenue (specifically, per-student
4:98:7124:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:program service revenue, a proxy for tuition) in response to unrestricted programs, but not for restricted
4:99:7230:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:programs. Thus both types of program raise school revenue, but in very different ways. For unrestricted
4:100:7333:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:programs we cannot reject that the incidence of the subsidy falls entirely on schools while for other laws we
4:101:7442:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:cannot reject that it falls entirely on families. These results are consistent with several depictions of private
4:102:7555:20:20:72:208:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:education, as we discuss below.
4:103:7586:20:20:86:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Finally, we construct a simple estimate of the price elasticity of demand for private schooling and a
4:104:7687:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:very simple estimate of the deadweight loss created for each dollar of public revenue spent on private-school
4:105:7796:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsidization. Our results suggest a price elasticity of -0.52; this is slightly larger in absolute value than
4:106:7906:20:20:72:540:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the baseline estimate in Dynarski, Gruber, and Li (2009), although one could argue that the population
4:107:8008:20:20:72:540:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:they study (families with siblings already in private school) would be less price sensitive than the population
4:108:8119:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:affected by publicly-funded subsidies that we consider here. Our simple estimate of deadweight loss is 3
4:109:8223:20:20:72:402:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:cents per dollar spent; we know of no comparable estimate for this number.
4:110:8297:20:19:86:539:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The rest of this paper is organized as follows. The next section describes the school subsidy laws used
4:111:8400:19:0:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:here. Section 3 discusses the data, section 4 the specifications, section 5 the results, and section 6 concludes.
5:112:8514:0:30:72:239:710:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:2.  School Subsidy Laws
5:113:8537:30:20:72:540:680:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The main estimates in this paper focus on what we refer to as “school subsidy laws.” By this, we mean
5:114:8638:20:20:72:540:660:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:laws that use government funds to subsidize private school tuition. This includes both traditional vouchers
5:115:8745:20:20:72:540:640:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and related tax-credit based subsidy laws; while technically distinct from traditional school vouchers, tuition
5:116:8856:20:20:72:540:620:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:tax credits serve the same purpose and work in a very similar way. A typical voucher program consists
5:117:8957:20:20:72:540:600:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of a government agency distributing money, which has been collected through taxation, to the families of
5:118:9061:20:20:72:540:580:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:students on the condition that the money will be spent on private schooling. In a typical tax credit program,
5:119:9170:20:20:72:540:560:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the government reduces the tax liability, often dollar for dollar up to some limit, of a taxpayer that has
5:120:9276:20:20:72:540:540:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:provided funding to support students’ private schooling. In both cases, states dedicate public resources to
5:121:9383:20:20:72:540:520:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:private school attendance for select students. The similarities between vouchers and tuition tax credits have
5:122:9492:20:20:72:540:500:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:led some to refer to these tax credits as “neovouchers” (Welner, 2008), or sometimes simply as “vouchers”
5:123:9597:20:19:72:540:480:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(e.g., Caputo, 2013). We will refer to these laws as subsidies and, when using the term vouchers hereafter,
5:124:9704:19:20:72:540:461:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:we will endeavor to clarify whether the term refers to subsidies generally or strictly to traditional voucher
5:125:9813:20:20:72:338:441:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:programs that use vouchers per se as a financing mechanism.
5:126:9872:20:20:86:539:421:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Our focus will be on several large statewide laws that affected reasonably large groups of students between
5:127:9979:20:20:72:540:401:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:1991 and 2009. With the exception of Chan (2006), who looks at the effect of a tax-credit law in Ontario on
5:128:10086:20:20:72:540:381:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:public school performance, and Rinz (2015), which explores how these laws affect student/teacher ratios and
5:129:10193:20:20:72:539:361:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:other educational inputs for students of different races, we know of no economic research on these laws.1The
5:130:10301:20:20:72:540:341:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:discussion here draws on discussions in Welner (2008), the Friedman Foundation (2013), Schaeffer (2009),
5:131:10405:20:20:72:520:321:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and a report compiled by the Research Department of the Minnesota House of Representatives (2011).
5:132:10503:20:20:86:539:301:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Tax-credit subsidy laws for private school tuition fall into two areas: credits for individuals and credits
5:133:10610:20:20:72:540:281:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for corporations. Corporate tax credit programs provide an income-tax credit for corporate donations to a
5:134:10715:20:20:72:540:261:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:school tuition organization, or STO, which then uses the donated funds to award scholarships to children
5:135:10819:20:20:72:539:241:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:attending private schools.2There are three states which enacted corporate tax credits of this kind during
5:136:10924:20:20:72:540:221:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the period of study here: Arizona, Florida, and Pennsylvania. These programs use credits, not deductions,
5:137:11029:20:19:72:540:201:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:so that the financial incentive created by the program can be quite large even if the state income tax rate is
5:138:11139:19:20:72:540:182:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:small. For example, in Florida during the time of this study the corporate income tax rate was 5.5%, but
5:139:11243:20:20:72:540:162:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Florida’s tax credit program awards a credit dollar-for-dollar with respect to donations to STOs. A program
5:140:11350:20:15:72:539:142:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in Florida that used a taxdeductionfor corporate donations would thus lower the opportunity cost of a
5:141:11451:15:12:83:539:127:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:1Some state-government reports consider the financial effect of these laws onpublicschool funding. That is not the focus of
5:142:11574:12:10:72:540:115:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:this paper, although it is certainly an important topic. Studies considering issues related to public finance and public education
5:143:11704:10:7:72:304:105:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:include Murray, Evans, and Schwab (1998) and Fischel (2006).
5:144:11764:7:12:83:540:98:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:2In recent years the tax base for some corporate credits has been expanded; for example in 2009 (beyond the period of study
5:145:11887:12:0:72:459:86:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:here) Arizona and Florida both expanded corporate tax credit programs to the insurance premiums tax.
6:146:11988:0:20:72:539:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:dollar given to STOs down from one dollar to 95 cents, but the taxcreditprogram lowers the cost of this
6:147:12091:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:donation from one dollar to zero. Indeed, with dollar-for-dollar credits the actual marginal income tax rate
6:148:12199:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is irrelevant. This extreme reimbursement rate creates a program that could in practice operate similarly
6:149:12304:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to a traditional voucher program, but the circuitous design of the program (having the subsidy delivered
6:150:12408:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:by way of a corporately-supported STO, rather than by the government directly) is attractive from a legal
6:151:12513:20:20:72:437:610:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:standpoint. We discuss the legal challenges to these subsidy programs more below.3
6:152:12595:20:20:86:539:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Individual income tax credit programs may either award a credit for donations made to STOs, or for a
6:153:12695:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:household’s own spending on private school tuition and other private school expenses. There are three states
6:154:12803:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with programs of this kind during the period of our study: Arizona, Iowa, and Illinois. Even though many
6:155:12907:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:state’s individual income tax rates are relatively low (compared to federal rates), these programs create large
6:156:13018:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:financial subsidies since they use credits rather than deductions. But most individual credits are different
6:157:13126:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:from other subsidy programs in that they both (a) allow individuals to claim credits for private school tuition
6:158:13237:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for their own children and (b) are not restricted to certain types of students, such as low income students,
6:159:13345:20:19:72:413:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:students with special educational needs, or students initially in public schools.
6:160:13426:19:20:86:539:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 1 provides information on the laws used in the main part of this study (we discuss several other
6:161:13528:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:laws momentarily).  There are eight laws that are the focus here: an individual and corporate credit in
6:162:13631:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Arizona, a voucher program and corporate credit in Florida, an individual credit in Illinois, two individual
6:163:13739:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:credits in Iowa (one restricted, one not), and a corporate credit in Pennsylvania.45As the table suggests,
6:164:13845:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:several programs are not means tested, and have relatively weak restrictions on student use. Appendix A
6:165:13948:20:20:72:348:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:provides some additional information on each state’s programs.
6:166:14010:20:20:86:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Figure 1 shows total spending each year from 1991 to 1999 on the programs listed in Table 1 (in year 2012
6:167:14115:20:15:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:dollars). Here, “spending” refers to (a) credits claimed for individual- and corporate-income tax programs
6:168:14221:15:13:83:540:276:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:3The Florida credit is capped at 75% of corporate taxes due. Arizona’s corporate income tax rate during the period of its
6:169:14342:13:9:72:540:263:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:corporate tax credit here was 6.968%; its program also awarded credits dollar-for-dollar. Pennsylvania’s program awarded a
6:170:14464:9:9:72:540:254:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:credit based on either 75% or 90% of a donation depending on whether the donation was a one time or repeated contribution;
6:171:14586:9:10:72:540:245:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:the fact that the credit is less than one-for-one means that the tax rate affects the cost of the donation. Pennsylvania’s corporate
6:172:14718:10:9:72:540:235:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:income tax rate during the period of study here was 9.99%. As of 2015, about 2,300 companies receive this Pennsylvania credit
6:173:14843:9:10:72:540:226:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:(see page D9 of Wolf, 2015) (this credit is capped at $300,000 a year during our period of study). Appendix A has more details
6:174:14969:10:7:72:124:216:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:on these laws.
6:175:14983:7:12:83:540:209:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:4There was a second voucher program in Florida that coincided with the McKay program, the A+ Accountability and
6:176:15094:12:9:72:540:197:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:School Choice Program. This program allowed students at public schools which had been awarded a grade of “F” in two of
6:177:15212:9:10:72:540:188:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:the prior 4 years to receive a voucher. Economists have studied the impact of this program on public school behavior; see
6:178:15333:10:9:72:540:178:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:Chakrabarti (2013) for a discussion. While the A+ program had the potential to influence public school behavior in interesting
6:179:15459:9:10:72:540:169:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:and important ways, the number of actual vouchers awarded under the program was much more limited than either of the
6:180:15575:10:9:72:540:159:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:programs in Table 1 (partly because of court rulings) and the program was ruled unconstitutional in 2006. In a typical year
6:181:15698:9:10:72:540:150:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:only a few hundred students used the program to attend private schools (Forster, 2008). Given that the program’s enrollment
6:182:15821:10:9:72:540:140:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:prior to 2001 (the year the corporate credit began) was even more limited (only two schools qualified for vouchers in the first
6:183:15948:9:10:72:540:131:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:possible year, 1999; and no schools did so in the second year) the baseline specifications below would be unchanged even if A+
6:184:16074:10:9:72:87:121:8:g_d0_f7:6:g_d0_f6:F:vouchers had become much more widespread in later years than they actually did (Greene, 2001).5
6:185:16169:9:10:87:540:112:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:In 2009, “Lexie’s Law” established a corporate tax credit in Arizona to fund STO scholarships specifically for students with
6:186:16293:10:9:72:540:102:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:disabilities. However, since corporate-tax-credit-financed STO scholarships with student eligibility restrictions already existed
6:187:16422:9:9:72:540:93:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:in Arizona and the size of this program was relatively small (in 2010 only about 100 students participated according to the
6:188:16545:9:10:72:540:84:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:Friedman Foundation, 2013), we treat Lexie’s Law as part of Arizona’s pre-existing restricted corporate tax credit program
6:189:16667:10:0:72:191:74:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:rather than as a separate entity.
7:190:16701:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and (b) traditional voucher spending under the McKay program. The figure shows that at the turn of the
7:191:16803:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:century there was a surge in school subsidies, with total funding reaching nearly half a billion dollars—a
7:192:16909:20:20:72:476:670:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:reasonably large amount given that these programs focus only on a select group of children.6
7:193:17001:20:20:86:539:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Figure 2 shows the number of individuals assisted by these laws. To count the number of individuals,
7:194:17101:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the figure sums three things: first, the number of scholarships awarded in each state and year by STOs
7:195:17203:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:that qualify for tax-credit status; second, the number of traditional vouchers paid out; and third, the total
7:196:17312:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:number of individual tax returns claiming a tax credit under a school-subsidy program. This figure may
7:197:17414:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:not exactly represent the number of students in school-subsidy policies, as the number of taxpayers claiming
7:198:17522:20:20:72:539:550:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:individual credits could differ from the number of students benefiting from the tax-exempted expenditures.7
7:199:17629:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:But the figure nonetheless clearly shows striking growth over the past two decades in participation in these
7:200:17737:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:programs, with over half a million individuals each year in the more recent years. The jump from 1999 to
7:201:17841:20:20:72:534:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:2000 reflects the expansion of several programs but is primarily driven by the Illinois program’s adoption.
7:202:17948:20:20:86:539:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:While Figures 1 and 2 show that the programs in Table 1 were large in terms of funding and scope, there
7:203:18051:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:were several other more limited programs enacted during the time period of available data here, particularly
7:204:18159:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:towards the end of the sample. Rhode Island introduced a corporate credit for donations to STOs in 2007,
7:205:18263:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:where a dollar of donations resulted in a 75 cent credit (or a 90 cent credit if donations are made in two
7:206:18369:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:consecutive years). But, aside from the fact that the program was adopted nearly at the end of the period
7:207:18474:20:20:72:398:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of study here, total eligible credits was capped at only $1 million annually.
7:208:18551:20:20:86:539:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:State voucher programs were also introduced in DC, Georgia, Ohio, and Utah.8The District of Columbia
7:209:18651:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Opportunity Scholarship program was launched in 2004 and provided vouchers for K-12 students who qualify
7:210:18755:20:20:72:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for free and reduced-price lunches, although the program had a “relatively small annual budget” (Friedman
7:211:18860:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Foundation, 2013). The Georgia Special Needs Scholarship program, enacted in 2007, allows any student
7:212:18961:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with a disability who was previously enrolled in public school to obtain a voucher to attend private school,
7:213:19069:20:20:72:540:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:although the program’s participation was relatively low—about a thousand students annually—for the period
7:214:19174:20:20:72:540:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:studied here. The Ohio Autism Scholarship Program, enacted in 2003, and later the Educational Choice
7:215:19274:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Scholarship Program in 2006, both provide vouchers to students across the state. The former program is
7:216:19376:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:limited only to students diagnosed with an autism spectrum disorder and registered in the public school
7:217:19479:20:19:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:special education system; participation in this program was relatively low during the period of study here.
7:218:19586:19:15:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The Educational Choice program provides vouchers to students who attend poorly-performing local public
7:219:19688:15:10:83:345:137:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:6Appendix Figure 1 provides a state-by-state breakdown of this figure.
7:220:19758:10:12:83:540:127:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:7The relevant individual-credit programs here limit credits to expenditures on dependents, so that very high discrepancies
7:221:19880:12:9:72:540:115:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:between credits claimed and students is unlikely. If multiple dependents benefit from a single taxpayer’s credit then the figure
7:222:20008:9:7:72:335:106:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:would underestimate the number of students assisted by these policies.
7:223:20078:7:12:83:540:99:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:8A voucher program was also considered in Colorado before being invalidated by a court ruling. Colorado has subsequently
7:224:20198:12:0:72:391:87:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:introduced a pilot voucher program in 2011, but this is after the period of study here.
8:225:20286:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:schools (or who attend a charter school but who would be assigned to such a school otherwise). Although
8:226:20389:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:participation was relatively small in the early years of the program (and the period of study used here), it
8:227:20497:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:has since become a large program with annual expenditures of nearly $60 million (Alliance for School Choice,
8:228:20605:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:2012). As these programs were more limited in scope or student availability during the years covered by the
8:229:20712:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:data below, we will exclude these programs. Many states have also passed related laws following the period
8:230:20818:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of analysis used here, including Colorado, Indiana, Louisiana, Mississippi, New Hampshire, North Carolina,
8:231:20924:20:20:72:230:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Oklahoma, Virginia, and Wisconsin.
8:232:20958:20:20:86:539:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The set of laws we study thus represents a fraction of the current set of laws. However, our laws have
8:233:21060:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:features in common with many subsequent programs.  For example, the corporate tax credit programs
8:234:21157:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:that we use appear to be good models for subsequently enacted programs that use tax credits to direct
8:235:21258:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:donations to third-party scholarship granting organizations, particularly with regard to means testing. Each
8:236:21366:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of these programs used here are means-tested; of the ten STO-style programs enacted since 2006, eight are
8:237:21471:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:means-tested. Moreover, many later voucher programs have important similarities with Florida’s McKay
8:238:21571:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:voucher program. Of the 17 subsequent voucher programs we know of that were enacted after the McKay
8:239:21670:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:program, nine are targeted at students with disabilities. Of those nine, six also have prior public school
8:240:21776:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:enrollment requirements that apply in at least some cases, so that McKay-style voucher programs account
8:241:21879:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for the majority of both voucher programs and programs for students with disabilities enacted after the
8:242:21982:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:cutoff for inclusion in our analysis. Student eligibility for the individual tax credit programs we include, on
8:243:22093:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the other hand, is unrestricted. The only subsequent programs with completely unrestricted eligibility are
8:244:22199:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsidies provided via reductions in individual income tax.9This of course does not necessarily mean that
8:245:22304:20:20:72:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:our results will be informative for all future law changes. But it is noteworthy that, while we use a limited
8:246:22413:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:number of laws, they contain features relevant to many subsequent laws, and we will (as discussed below)
8:247:22517:20:20:72:360:271:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:use subsequent adopters for robustness purposes in our analysis.10
8:248:22583:20:20:86:539:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:One might naturally wonder about the circumstances behind these states adopting large school subsidy
8:249:22683:20:20:71:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:laws before other states. As Table 1 suggests, the policies passed by states here include several relatively large
8:250:22797:20:20:71:539:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:states but feature states from across different parts of the country; the laws were signed both by democratic
8:251:22906:20:20:71:539:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and republican governors. Moreover, while five states are included, the number of states seriously pursuing or
8:252:23016:20:19:71:539:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsequently enacting similar school subsidy programs is much larger; Huerta and d’Entremont (2007) count
8:253:23121:19:15:71:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:at least 40 states considering tax credit programs along the lines of the programs used here.11Further, several
8:254:23232:15:13:83:540:137:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:9There are subsequent programs of this type in three states: Indiana, Louisiana, and Wisconsin. One program, enacted in
8:255:23351:13:9:72:540:124:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:Alabama in 2013, operates through the individual tax code and includes a student eligibility restriction. The program provides
8:256:23477:9:7:72:385:115:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:a refundable tax credit to parents who transfer children out of failing public schools.
8:257:23564:7:12:79:540:108:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:10The continued commonality in shared characteristics also suggests that, even if a fuller set of subsequent laws were available,
8:258:23693:12:7:72:453:96:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:parsimonious specifications would likely still be needed for estimations exploring heterogeneous effects.
8:259:23798:7:12:79:540:89:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:11Although they do not figure in our analysis, state charter school programs were also becoming widespread during and after
8:260:23921:12:0:72:540:77:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:our analysis period; all five states with tax credits we use in our main specifications also have charter school programs. With
9:261:24049:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:states have had attempted passage of programs blocked by court ruling or referenda, including California,
9:262:24154:20:20:72:539:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Colorado, Florida, Michigan, Vermont, and Washington.12These recent adopters can be exploited to create
9:263:24257:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:a more refined control-group for estimation below—that is, if estimates using “late” adopters as a control
9:264:24363:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:group yield highly different estimates than estimates using all non-Table-1 states as a control group, it
9:265:24468:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:would raise concerns that there are strong differences in private school markets that are related to (eventual)
9:266:24579:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:school-subsidy adoption. Similar estimates regardless of the control group used would lessen this concern.
9:267:24685:20:20:72:171:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:We explore this below.
9:268:24707:20:20:86:539:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:But while the large number of states eventually undertaking school subsidy programs can allow some
9:269:24805:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:investigation into the selectivity of states listed Table 1, one might wonder what determined the timing of
9:270:24912:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:law passage in different states. In some cases the timing stems in part from political events.13But the
9:271:25015:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:prominent force in the timing of these laws was perhaps judicial. School subsidy programs face important
9:272:25119:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:legal hurdles, with a foremost obstacle being the historical presence of Blaine Amendments in several state
9:273:25226:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:constitutions. In 1875, James Blaine, a congressional representative in Maine, proposed an amendment to
9:274:25329:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the constitution to preclude grants or appropriations to sectarian institutions or organizations.14While the
9:275:25437:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:amendment was not adopted (falling four votes short of adoption in the Senate), 37 states subsequently
9:276:25539:20:20:72:539:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:adopted “Blaine Amendments” to their constitutions.15The movement to adopt these amendments was
9:277:25634:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:often associated with anti-Catholic sentiment (Huerta and d’Entremont, 2007). Further, 29 states include a
9:278:25740:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:constitutional clause to prohibit the state from forcing residents to support any ministry (as states sometimes
9:279:25851:20:20:72:539:351:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:did in Colonial times). Only three states have neither a Blaine Amendment nor a compelled support clause.16
9:280:25958:20:20:86:539:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Blaine Amendments and anti-support clauses have historical roots that far precede the modern school-
9:281:26058:20:20:71:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:choice movement; to quote Duncan (2003), “the social and religious contexts in which the State Blaines
9:282:26160:20:20:71:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:operate today are far different from those of their origins.” But they have proven to be somewhat unpre-
9:283:26264:20:20:71:539:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:dictable determinants of the legality of school-subsidy policies today. Court decisions over the validity of
9:284:26372:20:17:71:539:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:these policies have varied. In Illinois, two separate cases on the legality of the state’s tax-credit program
9:285:26481:17:10:72:540:234:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:the exception of Iowa, each state’s charter program preceded its tax credit by at least three years. Given the differential timing
9:286:26611:10:9:72:540:224:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:of charter expansion, these programs should not drive the effects we report here; if anything, the availability of these alternative
9:287:26743:9:7:72:513:215:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:public-school programs would mitigate any effects of private-school subsidies on private school revenue and enrollment.
9:288:26862:7:12:79:540:208:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:12Additionally, in May 2013, Senator Marco Rubio (R-FL) introduced a bill to establish a federal tax credit that would be
9:289:26983:12:7:72:245:196:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:available to both individuals and corporations.
9:290:27030:7:12:79:540:189:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:13For example, Florida’s programs coincided with Jeb Bush’s election in 1998 after he narrowly lost his electoral bid in 1994,
9:291:27156:12:10:72:540:177:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:and the corporate tax credit in Arizona was made law by governor Janet Napolitano in a compromise with the state legislature
9:292:27280:10:9:72:540:167:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:during state budget negotiations after she had vetoed four such bills earlier (Welner, 2008). Tom Ridge signed Pennsylvania’s
9:293:27405:9:10:72:540:158:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:law after four failed attempts to get vouchers through the legislature (Averett and Wilkerson, 2001), although Adams (2002)
9:294:27528:10:9:72:86:148:8:g_d0_f7:6:g_d0_f6:F:refers the adoption of Pennsylvania’s credit as “quiet” and “little noticed.”14
9:295:27607:9:10:87:540:139:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:During his career, Blaine also served as Secretary of State, Speaker of the House, a Republican Presidential nominee, and
9:296:27728:10:9:72:86:129:8:g_d0_f7:6:g_d0_f6:F:a member of the Senate.15
9:297:27753:9:10:87:540:120:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:The wording of Blaine’s proposed amendment was, “No State shall make any law respecting an establishment of religion, or
9:298:27873:10:9:72:540:110:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:prohibiting the free exercise thereof; and no money raised by taxation in any State for the support of public schools, or derived
9:299:28002:9:9:72:540:101:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:from any public fund therefor, nor any public lands devoted thereto, shall ever be under the control of any religious sect; nor
9:300:28129:9:7:72:443:92:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:shall any money so raised or lands so devoted be divided between religious sects or denominations.”
9:301:28228:7:0:79:469:85:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:16The three states are Louisiana, North Carolina, and, unexpectedly, James Blaine’s own state of Maine.
10:302:28332:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:yielded six separate decisions before the legality of the program was recognized (Huerta and d’Entremont,
10:303:28437:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:2007). When Arizona enacted the first tax-credit school-subsidy program the legislature was in fact uncertain
10:304:28546:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of the program’s legality (Werner, 2008), although the legality was ultimate affirmed in the State Supreme
10:305:28652:20:20:72:539:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Court ruling ofKotterman V. Killianin 1999. But state-constitutional clauses on religion have provided
10:306:28754:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:limitations on school subsidies, as discussed in chapter 5 in Werner (2008). In some states where it appears
10:307:28862:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:that Blaine Amendment language may preclude subsidies, there have been attempts to alter the language of
10:308:28966:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the law.17Thus, the process for enacting subsidies can involve legislative and judicial deliberation, creating
10:309:29076:20:20:72:348:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:uncertainty on the timing of enactment within adopting states.
10:310:29138:20:20:86:539:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The decision of governments and courts to revisit legal provisions that have “slumbered in state con-
10:311:29239:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:stitutions for over a century” (Duncan, 2003) may in part coincide with other efforts to reform public
10:312:29341:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:education. This appears to be true in several of the states listed in Table 1. In particular, the establishment
10:313:29452:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of Florida’s McKay program coincided with an increase in public school funding for school construction
10:314:29554:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and maintenance, and Pennsylvania’s tax-credit program coincided with a program to support educational
10:315:29656:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:improvement organizations that provide innovative public school programs. However, if these coincidental
10:316:29760:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:laws made public schooling more attractive, they presumably workedagainstthe efficacy of laws intending
10:317:29863:20:20:72:309:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to facilitate student’s matriculation to private schools.
10:318:29920:20:20:86:539:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Another concern is simply one of reverse causation—growth in private school revenue may allow for
10:319:30017:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:private schools to devote lobbying resources towards school subsidies, so increases in private school revenue
10:320:30126:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:lead to greater school subsidies, not the other way around. Although such a coincidental timing pattern
10:321:30229:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:might be unlikely given the discussion above, we can address these concerns in the empirical part of the
10:322:30333:20:20:72:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:paper by including a number of controls for a state’s socioeconomic circumstances and the population of
10:323:30436:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:school-aged children. Additionally, we discuss empirical tests of this concern below. Before turning to the
10:324:30543:20:44:72:350:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:empirical methodology, however, we next discuss the data used.
10:325:30605:44:30:72:126:227:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:3.  Data
10:326:30613:30:20:72:540:197:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:This section discusses the data used; additional details are provided in the appendix. The primary financial
10:327:30721:20:20:72:540:177:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:data we use come from the Core Financial Files produced by the National Center for Charitable Statistics
10:328:30825:20:20:72:540:157:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(NCCS). These files contain information collected from Internal Revenue Service (IRS) Form 990 and Form
10:329:30928:20:20:72:540:137:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:990-EZ for 501(c)(3) public charities with at least $25,000 in receipts. Although these organizations are “non
10:330:31038:20:20:72:540:117:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:profit”, they must file tax returns containing detailed financial information. As mentioned in the last section,
10:331:31150:20:13:72:540:97:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:several states changed their laws in limited ways or very late in our sample (Ohio after 2003; Utah after 2005;
10:332:31261:13:12:79:540:84:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:17For example, in 2013 North Dakota considered a bill (HC3037) to alter the state’s constitution to make it easier to permit
10:333:31385:12:0:72:208:72:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:state support of sectarian education.
11:334:31423:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Georgia and Rhode Island after 2007; and Louisiana after 2008); we omit these state/year observations. The
11:335:31529:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:primary financial variable we use is program service revenue, which is revenue earned from activities that
11:336:31635:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:form the basis of an organization’s exemption from tax. Tuition charged by a private school is a canonical
11:337:31741:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:example, although some other revenue, such as from the sale of school supplies, would also be included in the
11:338:31850:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:figure we use. Using a figure that includes all program service revenue, rather than just tuition, is sensible
11:339:31960:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:as schools may potentially attempt to obtain school-subsidy rents by raising both tuition and other student
11:340:32067:20:20:72:539:590:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:fees.18It also reflects true revenue, as opposed to estimating revenue based on “sticker” tuition prices.19
11:341:32174:20:20:71:539:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Our NCCS data use tax returns covering fiscal years from 1991 to 2009. We know of no other large-scale
11:342:32276:20:20:71:453:550:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:annual dataset that provides this type of financial information for US private schools.20
11:343:32365:20:20:86:539:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:This nonprofit tax-return data includes information for all nonprofits filing such returns, not just schools.
11:344:32474:20:20:71:539:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:To identify private schools in the tax return data, we match the tax return data to information in the Private
11:345:32584:20:20:71:539:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:School Universe Survey (PSS). Conducted biennially by the National Center for Education Statistics, the
11:346:32687:20:20:71:539:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:PSS is intended to be a census of private schools in the United States, but it does not include any financial
11:347:32796:20:19:71:539:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:information about schools. We match the two datasets using information on school name and address. As
11:348:32897:19:20:71:539:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:discussed in the appendix, school names and addresses are only available in the PSS beginning in 2005/06
11:349:33001:20:20:71:539:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:but are available for all years in the NCCS data. This allows us to match any school in the PSS from 2005
11:350:33106:20:20:71:539:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:onwards, and then “follow” that school back in time. However, this procedure will miss schools that existed
11:351:33213:20:20:71:501:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:during the period of our sample (e.g., the 1990s) and exited the sample before the 2005/2006 PSS.
11:352:33310:20:20:86:539:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:There are several steps we can take to address this fact. First, we explored the degree of “churn” in
11:353:33411:20:20:71:539:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:our data to see if there is strong attrition as the sample goes back into the 1990s. Fortunately, this is not
11:354:33520:20:20:71:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the case, as over 70 percent of the observations in our matched sample can be followed back to 1995 and
11:355:33623:20:20:71:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:over half can be followed back to 1991. Next, we address this concern by identifying schools in the NCCS
11:356:33727:20:20:71:539:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:using additional information on charities’ tax returns. NCCS data include a charity’s National Taxonomy of
11:357:33833:20:20:71:539:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Exempt Entities (NTEE) Classification System code, which describes an organization’s primary purpose. We
11:358:33937:20:15:71:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:identify charities that classify themselves using codes B20 (elementary and secondary schools), B24 (primary
11:359:34045:15:12:79:540:216:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:18Other examples of program service revenue would include admissions fees charged by a museum, or conference registration
11:360:34166:12:7:72:293:204:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:fees charged by the ASSA for attending its annual meeting.
11:361:34224:7:12:79:540:197:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:19One may wonder how financial aid awarded by a school relates to reported program service revenue. There may be several
11:362:34344:12:9:72:540:185:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:ways that in practice private schools adjust program service revenue when awarding financial aid; it appears to us that reporting
11:363:34473:9:10:72:540:176:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:financial aid as reduced program service revenue is a much more common practice among schools than other possible alternatives.
11:364:34600:10:9:72:540:166:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:The vast majority of private schools (on the order of 95%) report providing financial aid. While the dataset in this paper does
11:365:34727:9:10:72:540:157:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:not provide information on expenditures comparable to the information available on revenue, we obtained a set of fuller 990
11:366:34850:10:9:72:540:147:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:forms for some of our sample via Morningstar, and in these cases positive grant expenditures are reported on Form 990 for
11:367:34971:9:10:72:540:138:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:only about 30% of school-years. If schools in our sample make financial aid available at the same rate as schools at large do,
11:368:35097:10:9:72:540:128:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:then most are reporting it as reduced program service revenue. In that case, decreases in aid in response to subsidies will be
11:369:35223:9:7:72:502:119:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:captured by as an increase in program service revenue (and revenue per student, all else equal) in the results below.
11:370:35340:7:12:79:540:112:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:20There are several concepts of “year” that are relevant here. As discussed in the appendix, the NCCS uses several notions
11:371:35462:12:10:72:540:100:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:of year when compiling the data; our use of the term reflects the fiscal year for which a charity files its tax return. The PSS
11:372:35589:10:9:72:540:90:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:timing is to October of a given year; we thus match NCCS and PSS data together based on the October in which the relevant
11:373:35710:9:9:72:540:81:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:fiscal year falls. Since most fiscal years are either calendar years or July-to-June fiscal years, this matching procedure should
11:374:35839:9:0:72:466:72:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:work well. We discuss a few additional issues with handling the timing of school subsidy laws more below.
12:375:35946:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and elementary schools), and B25 (secondary and high schools). The benefit of identifying schools in this
12:376:36051:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:way is that it avoids the bias towards schools that are open towards the end of the sample period.21Below,
12:377:36157:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:we show results using the PSS matched schools and then present estimates where additional NCCS-identified
12:378:36262:20:20:72:523:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:schools are added. The estimates are quite similar, suggesting that our matching procedure works well.
12:379:36364:20:20:86:539:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The populations of schools in the PSS and NCCS datasets are potentially different; most notably, some
12:380:36465:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:religiously affiliated private schools—particularly Catholic Schools—are not required to file tax returns and
12:381:36574:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:almost never voluntarily do so.  Our sample essentially excludes Catholic schools.  Our matched sample
12:382:36676:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:contains in an average year about 3,200 schools with about 800,000 students; or about one out of every nine
12:383:36783:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:private schools and one out of every five private-school students in a typical year. Appendix Table 1 shows
12:384:36890:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:our success rate in matching schools between the tax data and the PSS and shows that our sample consists
12:385:36994:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of schools that are larger and less likely to be religious than the average private school. However, the PSS
12:386:37102:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:data contain information on religious affiliation, and about half of all schools in the sample report that they
12:387:37213:20:20:72:532:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are religiously affiliated. Appendix Table 2, and its accompanying text, gives a state-by-state breakdown.
12:388:37319:20:19:86:539:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Nonetheless, one might wonder how the results for our sample of schools might extend to other schools.
12:389:37421:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:We will attempt to be careful in drawing strong conclusions for all schools from the results below. But we
12:390:37527:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:note that several of our results would be noteworthy even if they pertained to our sample alone. Further,
12:391:37632:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:there are several facts that give us optimism that our results might serve at least as a rough guide for the
12:392:37740:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:effects of subsidies on other schools. First, we can construct the ratio of students enrolled in schools filing a
12:393:37853:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:form 990 to all private-school students; if this ratio changes in response to the passage of a school subsidy, it
12:394:37966:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:might raise a concern that schools using subsidies concurrently decide to file. Estimating such a regression
12:395:38074:20:20:72:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:using the baseline specification below suggests that the fraction of filing schools doesnotchange when
12:396:38176:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsidies are introduced; the estimated impact of a subsidy on the fraction of students in filing schools is
12:397:38284:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:small, statistically insignificant, and produces a confidence interval ruling out a modest increase (or decrease)
12:398:38397:20:20:72:232:251:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:in the relative size of filing schools.22
12:399:38438:20:20:86:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Second, we can explore trends in teacher and enrollment populations in our sample and in the PSS overall;
12:400:38543:20:20:71:539:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:evidence of strongly divergent trends would also raise a concern about the generalizability of our sample.
12:401:38649:20:20:71:539:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Figure 3 shows trends over time in average enrollment (Panel A) and in average number of teachers (Panel
12:402:38753:20:19:71:539:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:B). Clearly the schools in our sample are larger and have more teachers. But the two panels show that in
12:403:38857:19:15:71:539:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:both samples there is a slight decline in enrollment over the period of study and that the number of teachers
12:404:38966:15:13:79:540:137:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:21One might ask why we do not simply construct the sample using NTEE codes, dispensing with the PSS entirely. First,
12:405:39082:13:9:72:540:124:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:some schools may not be identified under these codes, as discussed in the appendix. Second, some schools identified in this
12:406:39205:9:9:72:540:115:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:fashion cannot be matched to PSS data, so we cannot calculate revenue-per-student (the tax return information does not give
12:407:39328:9:7:72:352:106:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:the number of students in a school), which we use in some estimates below.
12:408:39402:7:12:79:540:99:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:22Using a specification matching column 5 of Table 6 below, where the dependent variable is the fraction of students in
12:409:39521:12:10:72:540:87:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:filing schools (mean of 0.199, standard deviation of 0.10), the coefficient on the School Subsidy Laws variable is 0.0044 and the
12:410:39650:10:0:72:440:77:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:state-clustered standard error is 0.0036, producing a 95% confidence interval from -0.0029 to 0.012.
13:411:39752:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is reasonably flat. The trends in these characteristics for schools in our sample seem reasonably comparable
13:412:39860:20:20:72:252:690:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:to the trends for all schools in the PSS.23
13:413:39903:20:20:86:539:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Third, for the year 2007, we obtained from Arizona school-by-school information on the amount of tax-
13:414:40004:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:credit-eligible scholarships claimed; using this information, we can explore whether the schools in our sample
13:415:40114:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:receive disproportionate amounts of subsidies. Evidence of this might affect the perceived generalizability of
13:416:40224:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:our results. In this particular state and year, the schools in our sample received about 18% of all tax-credit
13:417:40334:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:scholarships. We have 60 AZ schools in our sample this year while the PSS lists 334, so that the fraction
13:418:40439:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of schools in our sample (60/334 = 0.1796) matches the fraction of school subsidy funds captured by our
13:419:40542:20:20:72:150:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:sample quite well.
13:420:40560:20:20:86:539:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Finally, while we do not have financial information in the PSS, we do have enrollment, and below we
13:421:40659:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:can compare the estimated effect of enrollment for our sample to the effect for all schools. The precision
13:422:40765:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of the results (for both samples) can vary based on the construction of our standard errors, but fortunately
13:423:40873:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the estimates are qualitatively similar and suggest that the change in enrollment seen in our sample reflects
13:424:40982:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the change in enrollment for other private schools. We can also use this enrollment-based comparison to
13:425:41085:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:mediate our out-of-sample predictions for our financial results and below we do so. We will also use tuition
13:426:41193:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:information in the Schools and Staffing Survey to adjust these predictions; doing so suggests that our tax
13:427:41299:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:sample has reasonable predictive content for other private schools. In sum then, our sample includes only a
13:428:41406:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:fraction of private schools and these schools differ in certain observable ways from the typical private school.
13:429:41518:20:20:72:497:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:But we are optimistic that the results below may be informative for other private schools as well.
13:430:41616:20:20:86:539:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:After constructing our tax-return sample, we then drop several types of observations. First, we drop
13:431:41716:20:20:72:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:a small number observations with multiple matches between the PSS and NCCS. We also exclude a few
13:432:41813:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:schools that report being located in different states in different years, including any that fail to report a
13:433:41922:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:state in any year. We also drop tax returns for schools in outlying US territories, which we cannot match to
13:434:42030:20:20:72:540:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:enrollment records, since the PSS does not cover these territories. We exclude observations that report zero
13:435:42138:20:20:72:540:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:or negative program service revenue or do not report program service revenue on their tax returns. Lastly,
13:436:42244:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and perhaps most notably, the NCCS guidelines themselves note that the tax return dataset sometimes lists
13:437:42349:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:very large revenue values; we drop observations where revenue per student figures are extremely high; over
13:438:42455:20:19:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:$100,000. This amounts to about 4 percent of the sample. It is possible that a legitimate private school has
13:439:42563:19:20:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:per-student revenue of over $100,000 but we expect that most of these observations are erroneous, or may
13:440:42667:20:15:72:540:132:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:reflect the returns of parent organizations, such as a hospital or university, that offer education or daycare
13:441:42777:15:12:79:540:117:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:23Since the tax sample is less likely to be religiously affiliated, one might wonder if there are divergent trends in the share of
13:442:42907:12:10:72:540:105:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:the samples made up by religious schools. Appendix Figure 2 shows that from the mid 1990s on the two samples show similar
13:443:43028:10:9:72:540:95:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:trends (although again different levels) in the share of schools that are Catholic, religious-but-not-Catholic, or not religiously
13:444:43158:9:0:72:106:86:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:affiliated.
14:445:43171:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to children. Below we discuss estimates where this $100,000 cutoff is altered; the coefficients from samples
14:446:43279:20:45:72:424:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with different cutoffs are typically qualitatively similar to the baseline estimates.
14:447:43364:45:30:72:181:645:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:4.  Specification
14:448:43381:30:20:72:540:615:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:This section describes the specification we use for our baseline estimates. We first aggregate our sample to
14:449:43489:20:37:72:228:595:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the state/year level.24We estimate:
14:450:43524:37:36:207:403:558:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f10:C:ysy=αindexsy+βXsy+θs+θy+ Tsy+εsy
14:451:43556:36:20:72:540:522:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:where, for the baseline specification,ysyis total private-school revenue in statesin yearyin year 2012
14:452:43658:20:20:72:540:502:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:dollars, index is an index for school subsidy laws (described below), X is a matrix of regressors, including
14:453:43766:20:20:72:540:482:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:controls for state median income, the state unemployment rate, the population density, the fraction of a
14:454:43870:20:20:72:540:462:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:state’s residents that are non-citizens, the fraction born abroad, the total population, the population of
14:455:43976:20:20:72:540:442:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:white children ages 6-17, and the population of nonwhite children ages 6-17. These variables are taken from
14:456:44083:20:20:72:540:422:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the Bureau of Labor Statics, the Census, and the American Community Survey; see the appendix for details.
14:457:44188:20:20:72:540:402:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The termθsrepresents a set of state dummies,θyrepresents a set of year dummies, andTsyrepresents a set
14:458:44290:20:20:72:540:382:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of state-specific time trends and their coefficients; both linear and quadratic trends will be used. The term
14:459:44399:20:19:72:122:362:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f3:B:εsyis noise.
14:460:44411:19:20:86:539:343:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The index used in the baseline specification simply equals the number of different types of school subsidy
14:461:44517:20:20:72:540:323:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:laws present in a state: restrictive individual income-tax credits, unrestricted individual credits, corporate
14:462:44627:20:20:72:540:303:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:income tax credits (all are restricted), or traditional vouchers (all are restricted). The index takes on values
14:463:44739:20:20:72:540:283:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:zero (no school subsidy), one (one school subsidy program) or two (two types of school subsidy program, true
14:464:44847:20:20:72:540:263:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in Florida after 2001, Arizona after 2006, and Iowa after 2006). However, estimates will also be presented
14:465:44953:20:20:72:332:243:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:allowing different types of program to have different effects.
14:466:45015:20:20:86:539:223:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:This specification will allow us to capture effects of subsidies both on intensive margins (existing schools
14:467:45123:20:20:72:540:203:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:realizing changed outcomes) and extensive margins (the number of schools in communities changing) that
14:468:45225:20:20:72:540:183:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:would be missed by (e.g.) school-level regressions with school fixed effects. Also, the policy variation we
14:469:45332:20:20:72:540:163:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:focus on is at the state/year level. Further, this specification will motivate the additional results below based
14:470:45445:20:20:72:540:143:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:on school-level regressions, regressions on the number of schools, and extensive-versus-intensive effects, and
14:471:45555:20:14:72:540:123:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:this specification also facilitates our discussion of elasticities and welfare effects. But an obvious caveat to
14:472:45667:14:13:79:540:109:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:24As discussed in the appendix, the number of fiscal year 1998 NCCS returns is quite small, apparently because of a regulatory
14:473:45793:13:9:72:540:96:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:change in the dissemination of IRS 990 forms that year. After aggregating the data, we linearly impute revenue figures for fiscal
14:474:45922:9:0:72:110:87:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:year 1998.
15:475:45934:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:this specification is that our programs vary by size, so that the coefficients must be interpreted with this
15:476:46042:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:variation in program size in mind. We also report results using logged outcome variables below; the use of
15:477:46148:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:logs helps to mitigate any effects of variety in program size. Fortunately, the results from logged regressions
15:478:46259:20:20:72:348:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are similar to the baseline results using the above specification.
15:479:46325:20:20:86:539:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:In constructing the index, it can be unclear whether a law enacted in a certain year would first have
15:480:46426:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:an impact in the current school year or instead would impact subsequent school years. For our baseline
15:481:46528:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:regressions, we create our index based on the year a school subsidy program was enacted, but, to account for
15:482:46636:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the uncertainty in the timing of donations following enactment, include a dummy variable that equals unity
15:483:46742:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the year a state enacts a school-subsidy law (and zero all other years). There are a few exceptions to the
15:484:46848:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:basic rule of coding a law based on year of enactment. First, Iowa’s first law was enacted in 1987—before
15:485:46953:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:our sample begins—but was expanded in 1996 and again in 1998. State administrative data show a large
15:486:47053:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:increase in the program after the 1998 reform; we consequently code Iowa’s index as going from zero to one
15:487:47159:20:20:72:539:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in 1998.25Next, Arizona’s individual credit was enacted in 1997, but donations were not eligible for a credit
15:488:47268:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:until 1998, and use of the program was limited until 1999 (Arizona Department of Revenue, 2008). We code
15:489:47372:19:20:72:193:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Arizona’s first year as 1998.
15:490:47401:20:20:86:539:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Finally, while the McKay voucher program began in 1999, it was initially a pilot program; the program
15:491:47502:20:20:72:539:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:saw a rapid expansion in 2001.26We thus code both Florida programs beginning in 2001. Altering the
15:492:47600:20:20:71:539:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:timing of these laws to their earlier enactments, or simply dropping the year of enactment and the year
15:493:47703:20:20:71:507:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:following enactment from the regressions for all states, yields similar estimates to those shown here.
15:494:47805:20:20:86:539:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The specification above can be interpreted as a “difference-in-difference” style estimate. States adopting
15:495:47911:20:20:71:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:school subsidy laws are allowed to differ from other states, and can even have different trends over time given
15:496:48022:20:20:71:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:our trend controls. The specifications also account for changing economic and demographic trends across
15:497:48125:20:20:71:539:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:states and over time. However, the number of treatment states (five) is relatively small. As Conley and
15:498:48228:20:20:71:539:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Taber (2011) note, inference with difference-in-difference style estimates using a small number of treatment
15:499:48336:20:20:71:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:groups can be problematic, as estimates can be subject to a finite-sample-bias. They propose an inference
15:500:48441:20:20:71:539:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:technique to account for the small number of treatment groups in the estimation. In the results that follow,
15:501:48549:20:20:71:539:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:we both report conventional state-clustered standard errors, as well as 95-percent confidence intervals, using
15:502:48659:20:19:71:539:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:an approach based off of Conley and Taber’s method. We discuss the method in the appendix but provide
15:503:48760:19:37:71:416:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:a synopsis here. First, we estimate, using OLS on the full sample, the equation
15:504:48839:37:16:232:378:115:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f10:C:ysy=βXsy+θs+θy+ Tsy+εsy
15:505:48862:16:9:79:540:99:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:25Program expenditures tripled from 1997 to 1998, going from $3 million to $9 million (Iowa Department of Revenue, 2012).
15:506:48983:9:12:79:540:90:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:26Enrollment grew from 970 students in 00/01 to 5,013 in the 01/02 school year (Florida Office of Independent Education &
15:507:49104:12:0:72:159:78:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:Parental Choice, 2006).
16:508:49129:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:where the index coefficient has been removed, and create residuals  ̃εsy. We next assign the treatment states’
16:509:49239:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:index values to the control states, where the assignment is done at the state level (e.g., the index values
16:510:49346:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for all years in Arizona are assigned to Alabama). After the five true state-index profiles have randomly
16:511:49451:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:been assigned to five new states, we regress the new-states’ residuals  ̃εsyon the state-index profiles, state
16:512:49561:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and year dummies, and record the coefficient. We run 5,000 such regressions to estimate a distribution of
16:513:49666:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:coefficients that “should” be zero, but may be nonzero or non-symmetric due to finite sample bias. Using the
16:514:49774:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:null-hypothesis that the true coefficient value for the actual-law-change regression is zero, we can “invert”
16:515:49883:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:this distribution to construct a 95-percent confidence interval that takes the finite sample bias into account.
16:516:49994:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The confidence intervals produced by this approach are often similar to those suggested by the standard
16:517:50097:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:errors. But, particularly when the number of treatment states in a specification is very small (e.g., three),
16:518:50206:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the Conley-Taber approach can produce much less precise results. This may partly reflect limited statistical
16:519:50314:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:power of their test–in Table 3 of their paper they report Monte Carlo results where, for a very small number
16:520:50422:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of law changes, the power of their test (the ability of their test to reject a null that is false) is poor, with a
16:521:50536:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:genuinely false null rejected (at the 5% level) only 30 percent of the time with two law changes. We explored
16:522:50645:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:several asymptotically equivalent ways of constructing Conley-Taber style confidence intervals and here we
16:523:50751:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:report theleastprecise; other Conley-Taber-based approaches (see the appendix) provided greater precision.
16:524:50857:20:20:71:539:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Fortunately, many of the key results below are similarly precise either using standard errors or the alternate
16:525:50967:20:20:71:176:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:CT confidence intervals.
16:526:50991:20:20:86:539:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 2 provides selected means for variables of interest.  The first row, total revenue, reflects total
16:527:51095:20:20:71:539:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:program service revenue added up for all schools in a given state and year; the mean of this variable in
16:528:51199:20:20:71:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the first column of the table is the mean of aggregated total revenue, averaged over all states and years.
16:529:51305:20:20:71:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The typical observation in our sample thus records a little over $200 million in private school revenue. The
16:530:51413:20:20:71:539:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:means given in column 2 are for the 62 state/year combinations where school subsidies were greater than
16:531:51516:20:20:71:539:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:zero. Total revenue is somewhat smaller in observations with unrestricted programs, as shown in the last
16:532:51620:20:20:71:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:column. The second row, total enrollment, refers to total enrollment in our matched sample. The third
16:533:51721:20:20:71:539:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:row is unweighted revenue per student, suggesting per student revenue of about $10,000 across our sample.
16:534:51826:20:20:71:539:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The typical school has about 238 students, reflecting (as in Figure 3) that tax-sample schools are slightly
16:535:51933:20:19:71:539:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:larger than the average private school overall.  “Spending on School Subsidies” is constructed as before,
16:536:52038:19:20:71:539:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:reflecting vouchers, individual credits and corporate credits. The final row is subsidies per “user” where user
16:537:52149:20:20:71:539:132:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is constructed as in Figure 2. As noted earlier, unrestricted programs are smaller than the average subsidy
16:538:52256:20:20:71:539:112:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:program; Table 2 shows that they are about half the size of the average program. We will keep this in mind
16:539:52362:20:0:71:232:92:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:when discussing the estimates below.
17:540:52400:0:34:72:161:710:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:5.  Estimates
17:541:52413:34:26:72:208:676:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:C:5A. Baseline Estimates
17:542:52435:26:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Before turning to regression estimates, Figure 4 shows the basic result by plotting out total revenue before
17:543:52543:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and after a state first imposes a school subsidy law. We calculate average private-school revenue in year
17:544:52648:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:2012 dollars in our treatment states four years before a school law changed, three years before, and so on,
17:545:52755:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:up to four years after. Of course, in theory it is possible that the revenue effect will be zero. But the figure
17:546:52867:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:shows a clear and persistent jump in total revenue following the law change. Accounting for the pre-existing
17:547:52975:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:upward trend before the law change, the size of this jump appears to be about $30 million to $40 million in
17:548:53082:20:19:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:magnitude. We interpret a jump of this size below. It is also noteworthy that there is an absence of any clear
