17:549:53192:19:20:72:528:511:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:jump up or down in the year prior to the laws’ enactment (although we can test for this more formally).
17:550:53295:20:20:86:539:491:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 3 shows baseline regression results. The dependent variable is total program service revenue, in
17:551:53397:20:20:72:540:471:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:$1000s, for all schools in a state/year. Coefficients for the school-policy index are shown in each column,
17:552:53504:20:20:72:540:451:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:below that is the state-clustered standard error and below that the 95% confidence interval as adapted
17:553:53606:20:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:from Conley and Taber (2011). The first column shows that, when an additional school subsidy policy is
17:554:53708:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:introduced (increasing the index’s value by 1), total program service revenue in our sample increases by
17:555:53812:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:about $25 million. This is about 10 percent of average revenue (slightly more for all observations, slightly
17:556:53920:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:less for those with a subsidy) as shown in Table 2. Typical school-subsidy spending is about $60 million
17:557:54024:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:annually; the estimates indicate that–within our sample alone–this spending raises revenue substantially.
17:558:54129:20:20:72:404:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:We take up the extension of these effects to all private schools momentarily.
17:559:54206:20:20:86:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The rest of the table explores this result under other specifications. Column 2 removes the right-hand-side
17:560:54313:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:controls, but this does not alter the estimates. In column 3, we include only the sample of states that currently
17:561:54426:20:20:72:539:271:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:have school-subsidy laws, that had them prior to our sample, or that adopted them after our sample ends.27
17:562:54532:20:19:71:539:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:If the policy changes we are studying are from a highly selected group, then this alteration of our control
17:563:54639:19:20:71:539:232:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:group could lead to different estimates. Fortunately, this is not the case, and comparing the states that
17:564:54744:20:20:71:539:212:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:made the policy changes we use here to those that adopted similar policies just a few years later does not
17:565:54850:20:20:71:539:192:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:change the estimates. The next column adds quadratic (rather than just linear) state-specific trends, but the
17:566:54959:20:20:71:539:172:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:estimate is not sensitive to this change.28Column 5 adds in schools identified using the NTEE Classification
17:567:55067:20:15:71:539:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:System code, rather than just crosswalking schools between the PSS and the NCCS datasets. This raises
17:568:55168:15:12:79:540:137:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:27At the suggestion of a referee, we also redid the baseline estimate using only states with court-blocked programs (mentioned
17:569:55294:12:10:72:540:125:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:in the text in Section 2) as a control. These estimates are often similar to the estimates here, but feature a much smaller sample
17:570:55424:10:9:72:86:115:8:g_d0_f7:6:g_d0_f6:F:and are (unsurprisingly) sometimes less precise.28
17:571:55474:9:10:87:540:106:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:It is possible to obtain results similar to ours without the use of time trends, but these results can be sensitive to changes
17:572:55600:10:9:72:540:96:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:in the specification and can be less precise. Typically, however, results based on variation closer to our law cutoffs (e.g., results
17:573:55733:9:10:72:540:87:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:without trends that limit the sample to years near the law changes, omitting the influence of secular trends in the data far
17:574:55857:10:0:72:358:77:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:before or after the law changes) produce results closer to the main estimates.
18:575:55937:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the number of individual school/year observations in the sample from about 61,000 to about 78,000. If the
18:576:56042:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:estimates change significantly here, it might raise concerns over the sample selection process of matching PSS
18:577:56152:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and NCCS data. In fact, in this case the result is somewhat larger in magnitude.29Overall, Table 3 indicates
18:578:56260:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:that the average subsidy program raised revenue by about $30 million, a number roughly comparable to the
18:579:56364:20:20:72:175:630:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:jump shown Figure 4.30
18:580:56386:20:20:86:539:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Appendix Table 3 presents an additional robustness check of this result; the first column in that appendix
18:581:56492:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:table repeats the baseline estimate in Table 1 but adds in five “lead” dummies that equal zero (e.g.) one
18:582:56597:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:year before a school-subsidy law is enacted and zero all other years. The table shows there is no evidence of
18:583:56706:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:pre-existing trends in revenue, revenue per student, or total students in states that passed a school subsidy
18:584:56815:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:law, suggesting that the results here are not driven by reverse-causation or by some other unobservable driver
18:585:56925:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in these states. The last three columns add post-enactment lags as well. Looking at these coefficients, there
18:586:57034:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:does not appear to be a clear pattern (at least within the first few years) of program growth across outcomes,
18:587:57144:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:although the diminishing (in absolute value) post-lag coefficients for enrollment in the last column suggest
18:588:57252:20:19:72:323:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:that enrollment effects may increase somewhat over time.
18:589:57308:19:20:86:539:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 4 presents an alternate test of this possibility. As mentioned above, the tax returns include NTEE
18:590:57412:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:codes which identify an organization’s primary purpose. In Table 4 we estimate regressions on total revenue
18:591:57519:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for a variety of nonprofits that we do not expect to respond to school subsidies.  As mentioned in the
18:592:57621:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:appendix, many schools in the PSS may not be designated as schools by the NTEE. Using our data matched
18:593:57723:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to the PSS, we counted the number of PSS schools within each NTEE code, and in Table 4 report results
18:594:57824:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for the six NTEE categories with few (less than 30, although alternate cutoffs give similar results) matches
18:595:57932:20:20:72:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in the PSS. These regressions use the baseline estimations shown in column 1 of Table 3. Revenues are
18:596:58033:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:again in 1000s of 2012 dollars and as before groups with zero or negative revenues are excluded along with
18:597:58139:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:organizations with revenue above the 99th percentile in each code. Ifalltypes of nonprofit organizations,
18:598:58244:20:20:72:540:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:such as zoos and aquariums or voter registration groups, saw a surge in revenue that coincided with school
18:599:58350:20:20:72:540:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsidy laws, this would raise a concern that the increase we identify might be driven by some coincidental
18:600:58457:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:growth in nonprofits generally. But the findings in Table 4 indicate that this is not the case. The estimates
18:601:58566:20:20:72:393:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are all close to zero and, with the exception of public safety, insignificant.
18:602:58644:20:19:86:539:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 5 explores the effect of school subsidies across different types of program. In particular, we explore
18:603:58752:19:15:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:whether programs that restrict what students may use the subsidies differ from unrestricted programs. Table
18:604:58859:15:13:79:540:137:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:29As mentioned earlier, using different revenue-per-student cutoffs provide similar estimates for the results in Table 3. For
18:605:58984:13:9:72:540:124:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:example, using the full sample regardless of per-student revenue yields a baseline estimate for total revenue of 43,779 [std err
18:606:59112:9:9:72:540:115:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:= 28,586]. In contrast, limiting the sample to schools that have less than $25,000 in revenue per student yields total revenue
18:607:59238:9:7:72:178:106:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:coefficient of 43,867 [17,456].
18:608:59269:7:12:79:540:99:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:30Redoing column 1 with logged revenue, the coefficient of interest becomes 0.083 [se = 0.38], suggesting an effect close to
18:609:59393:12:0:72:191:87:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:the that in the levels regression.
19:610:59429:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:5 thus adds to the regressions in Table 3 an additional variable that is simply a dummy which equals unity
19:611:59535:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:if a state had an unrestricted subsidy program in effect. The first row of the table thus reflects the states
19:612:59644:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with corporate credit laws, along with one traditional voucher law (the McKay voucher in Florida). The
19:613:59746:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:unrestricted dummy will indicate how unrestricted laws differ, and the sum of these two coefficients will
19:614:59851:20:20:72:398:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:show the absolute (rather than the relative) effect of the unrestricted laws.
19:615:59928:20:20:86:539:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The first column, which repeats the baseline regression in Table 3 with the new dummy variable added,
19:616:60029:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:suggests that the increase in school revenue is smaller for unrestricted programs than for restricted programs.
19:617:60140:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:However, as noted in Table 2, unrestricted programs are also typically smaller (about half of the size) in
19:618:60246:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:terms of dollars spent, and later specifications in Table 5 suggest that the impact of unrestricted programs
19:619:60354:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is about half the size of the other programs, indicating that the dollar-for-dollar impact of these programs
19:620:60462:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:may be comparable. Further, while the standard errors on the unrestricted coefficients suggest a significant
19:621:60570:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:difference in several specifications, the confidence intervals are less precise. The imprecision may in part
19:622:60678:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:reflect power problems for the CT method when the number of treatment groups is very small, as noted
19:623:60778:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:earlier. In any case, Table 5 suggests that the dollar-for-dollar effect of unrestricted subsidies may be similar
19:624:60891:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to or perhaps smaller than other school subsidy programs. As in Table 3, the results are qualitatively similar
19:625:61001:20:20:72:222:411:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:across a variety of specifications.31
19:626:61038:20:20:86:539:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Looking at revenue per student will allow us to see whether schools’ increased revenue is derived by
19:627:61138:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:increasing enrollment, or by raising tuition & other fees, or both.32Table 6 presents results on the incidence
19:628:61248:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of school subsidies by exploring how subsidies affect per-student revenue, both for all schools in the sample
19:629:61357:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:combined and by type of law. Since these regressions, unlike those in Tables 3, 4, and 5, are per-student, they
19:630:61468:20:20:72:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are weighted by number of students. (Unweighted estimates are similar.) The baseline estimate is statistically
19:631:61578:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:insignificant. Its point estimate suggests that schools increase per-student revenue (e.g., by raising tuition)
19:632:61689:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:by a little over $250 when a new school subsidy program is introduced. Perhaps the closest estimate to this
19:633:61796:20:20:72:540:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in the literature is in Angrist, Bettinger, Bloom, King, and Kremer (2002), who find that winning a lottery
19:634:61903:20:20:72:540:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in Bogot ́a for a voucher worth $190 raised average private-school tuition and fees by $52, so that every dollar
19:635:62015:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of voucher funding raised tuition and fees by about 25 cents, close to what the point estimate here suggests.
19:636:62124:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:We note, however, that their research design compares voucher-lottery winners and losers. This approach,
19:637:62228:20:19:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:while excellent for the focus of their paper, could miss market changes in response to vouchers affecting all
19:638:62337:19:15:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:students, so that comparability of the estimates is ex ante unclear. The estimate here should be interpreted
19:639:62445:15:13:79:540:137:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:31When using logged expenditures, the baseline index coefficient becomes 0.076 [0.37] and the unrestricted dummy becomes
19:640:62565:13:9:72:540:124:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:0.028 [0.136]. This suggests similar proportional effects between the types of programs (perhaps slightly larger for unrestricted)
19:641:62695:9:7:72:475:115:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:which would fit with the results in Table 5 given that the revenue bases for the two types of programs differ.
19:642:62805:7:12:79:540:108:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:32If schools price discriminate, than increases in actual tuition paid might occur even if the sticker price remains unchanged;
19:643:62932:12:9:72:540:96:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:this is an advantage of our measure. If schools respond to subsidies by cutting actual prices to some students, or increasing
19:644:63057:9:0:72:352:87:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:prices for others, our measure will capture the net effect of these behaviors.
20:645:63137:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:as reflecting changes in price from several channels, including: schools price discriminating against voucher
20:646:63246:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:students, schools raising tuition and fees generally, marginal students entering the private market choosing
20:647:63354:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:schools with different prices than the average private-school student, and infra-marginal students choosing
20:648:63461:20:20:72:209:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:more expensive private schools.
20:649:63492:20:20:86:539:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The next column separates out unrestricted laws, and here the point estimates are different: the coefficient
20:650:63600:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:suggests that unrestricted laws see an increase in per-student revenue relative to other laws. While the CT
20:651:63707:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:confidence interval is large, the standard error for the unrestricted coefficient indicates the differential impact
20:652:63822:20:20:72:539:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of these laws is statistically significant.33Further, a Wald test of the hypothesis that the sum of the two
20:653:63929:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:coefficients is zero is rejected at the one percent level, suggesting that unrestricted laws raise prices absolutely
20:654:64045:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(and not just relatively). The implied increase in per student revenue in response to an unrestricted program
20:655:64154:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is over $1000 per student and reasonably close to our estimate of subsidy-funding-per-user noted at the end
20:656:64261:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of the prior section. Thus we cannot reject the hypothesis that the incidence of the subsidy falls entirely
20:657:64368:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:on families for the restricted laws, but wecanreject full incidence on families for unrestricted laws, where
20:658:64476:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the estimates are compatible with schools capturing the entire subsidy. The data are compatible with the
20:659:64580:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:incidence of subsidies falling entirely on schools for one type of law and entirely on families for another type
20:660:64692:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of law. The following columns consider similar robustness tests as before (the table omits results adding
20:661:64797:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:NCCS-identified schools to the sample as student counts are unavailable for these schools and the number
20:662:64901:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of students is now used in the dependent variable). In column 4, a Wald test again rejects that the sum of
20:663:65007:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the coefficients is zero and the combined coefficients, while slightly smaller than before, are still reasonably
20:664:65119:20:20:72:267:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:close to the overall subsidy-per-user average.
20:665:65165:20:20:86:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Together, Tables 5 and 6 suggest potentially similar dollar-for-dollar effects of subsidies on revenue across
20:666:65274:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:different programs but potentially different dollar-per-student effects, indicating that the laws have different
20:667:65386:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:effects on enrollment. Table 7 shows results with total enrollment in private schools in a state and year as
20:668:65494:20:20:72:540:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the dependent variable; these results confirm the findings in Tables 5 and 6. In column 2, the corporate
20:669:65598:20:20:72:540:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:credit programs and McKay voucher see an increase in student enrollment of about 2,000 students. The
20:670:65698:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:average statewide private school enrollment for observations with a restricted subsidy is a little over 20,000,
20:671:65809:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:so the coefficient indicates a reasonably large increase of about 10% in enrollment which is clearly statistically
20:672:65923:20:19:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:significant. This effect is smaller than the simulated prediction of a roughly one-third increase in enrollment
20:673:66034:19:20:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:from a $1000 voucher in Ferreyra (2007), although her modeling of the voucher is different from here and she
20:674:66142:20:20:72:539:132:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:assumes full mobility of households.34Further, the results for the unrestricted laws are strikingly different;
20:675:66252:20:15:72:540:112:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the estimates indicate that these laws saw zero enrollment growth and standard errors indicate that this
20:676:66356:15:12:79:540:97:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:33The logged revenue-per-student result is similar, with the index coefficient at 0.008 (se = 0.032) and the unrestricted dummy
20:677:66483:12:7:72:172:85:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:coefficient at 0.089 (0.038).
20:678:66512:7:0:79:244:78:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:34See the first row in Table 5A of her paper.
21:679:66559:0:20:72:456:710:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:difference is statistically significant.35Using quadratic trends produces similar results.36
21:680:66651:20:20:86:539:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The last two columns of Table 7 report estimates using total enrollment from the Private School Universe
21:681:66755:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Survey as the dependent variable. The sample size is smaller as the PSS is only biennial. The specification is
21:682:66865:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the same as in columns 1 and 2 except that the year-of-enactment dummy is also set to 1 for the year following
21:683:66975:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:enactment. The coefficient on restricted programs is a little over 3 times as large in the PSS sample.37While
21:684:67084:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the standard errors are larger, the CT confidence intervals again suggest an increase for school subsidy laws.
21:685:67194:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The coefficients suggest that our tax sample may have slightly larger increases in enrollment than would
21:686:67298:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:be the case if the sample were perfectly representative, although the precision of the estimates certainly
21:687:67404:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:would allow for the tax sample to be representative. Moreover, the estimates in the last two columns are
21:688:67508:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:qualitatively similar to the results using the tax return data, with restricted subsidies increasing enrollment
21:689:67619:20:20:72:256:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:much more clearly than unrestricted ones.
21:690:67660:20:20:86:539:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:In summary, the above estimates suggest that school-subsidy laws have reasonably large effects on private
21:691:67765:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:school revenue. However, we find evidence that the mechanisms by which revenue increases are different
21:692:67867:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:across types of laws; in particular we find that unrestricted laws lead to higher per-student revenue with
21:693:67973:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:little evidence of any change in student enrollment.  In the appendix (Figures 3, 4, and 5) we provide
21:694:68075:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:simple state-by-state graphical evidence of these results.  The figures show moving averages of revenue,
21:695:68179:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:revenue-per-student, and enrollment before and after a state first adopts a subsidy. The data show revenue
21:696:68285:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:jumps for all states (except perhaps Iowa); the jumps for restricted subsidies are more visually compelling
21:697:68392:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(although again their revenue bases and programs are on average relatively larger). Similarly, the unrestricted
21:698:68503:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:states see clear enrollment jumps, while the overall revenue-per-student results are (unsurprisingly) more
21:699:68609:20:20:72:479:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:mixed. A simple visual inspection across the states fits well with the estimations shown here.
21:700:68703:20:20:86:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Why do the effects of these laws differ? There are a few possible explanations. First, it may be that
21:701:68804:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:private schools enjoy a greater degree of market power for students they have already enrolled who then
21:702:68907:20:20:72:540:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:qualify for an unrestricted subsidy. Importantly, unrestricted laws allow families to target funding towards
21:703:69015:20:20:72:540:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:children already in private schools, including their own children, while other laws place restrictions on
21:704:69120:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:student eligibility based on income, disability, or a student’s prior enrollment. For the case of unrestricted
21:705:69230:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:laws, schools with relatively high market power can thus raise their tuition or other fees to capture the rent
21:706:69340:20:14:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:created by the subsidy. In contrast, the market may be more competitive for disabled students in public
21:707:69443:14:13:79:540:157:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:35The Tables 6 and 7 estimates are typically similar when including only schools with per-student revenue below $25,000 or
21:708:69565:13:9:72:540:144:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:when including all schools. The only notable difference is that, when using only schools with less than $25,000 per student
21:709:69688:9:10:72:540:135:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:revenue in the sample, both restricted and unrestricted subsidies lead to higher revenue-per-student, with the coefficient in
21:710:69813:10:9:72:540:125:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:column 1 in Table 6 becoming 0.65 [0.20]. But the results in the second column of Table 7, that there is a positive enrollment
21:711:69939:9:7:72:399:116:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:effect for restricted programs and no such effect for unrestricted, is found in all samples.
21:712:70031:7:12:79:540:109:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:36Again, estimates using logs are qualitatively similar; using logged enrollment here produces an index coefficient of 0.079
21:713:70155:12:7:72:274:97:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:(0.30) and an unrestricted coefficient of -0.092 (0.073).
21:714:70212:7:12:79:540:90:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:37While small, the unrestricted effect (the difference 5516 - 5477 in column 6 versus 1951 - 1944 in column 2) is a little over
21:715:70339:12:0:72:171:78:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:5 times larger for the PSS.
22:716:70368:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:school or low income students in public school who subsequently become interested in attending private
22:717:70470:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:school after qualifying for a restricted scholarship. If the marginal cost of adding one of these students is
22:718:70579:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:low, it is possible that schools may compete for these latter students by increasing enrollment with minimal
22:719:70687:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:changes in tuition or other student fees. A difference in market power could thus reconcile the estimates
22:720:70792:20:20:72:93:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:here.
22:721:70797:20:20:86:539:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:A second, non-exclusive, explanation is based on information. If schools want to cream skim potential
22:722:70898:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:entrants, the nature of this skimming might vary between the different laws.  When restricted subsidies
22:723:71001:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are provided, e.g., via STO organizations, schools may partner with these organizations to identify and
22:724:71104:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:vet attractive students. With unrestricted subsidies provided via individual credits, this screening device is
22:725:71214:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:unavailable. If schools are faced with an increase in demand following an unrestricted subsidies’ implementa-
22:726:71323:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:tion, and if (for example) family income is positively related to the unobservable characteristics that schools
22:727:71434:20:37:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:want to select on, then schools may subsequently be able to raise tuition to improve their screening process.
22:728:71543:37:26:72:327:453:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:C:5B. Intensive and Extensive Margin Effects
22:729:71585:26:20:72:540:427:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:The above specifications use aggregate data from all schools to capture effects from existing schools realizing
22:730:71696:20:20:72:540:407:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:different outcomes along with the composition of schools changing over time. In Table 8, we examine the
22:731:71799:20:20:72:540:387:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:importance of these different channels. The first three columns of panel A limit the aggregated sample to
22:732:71904:20:20:72:540:367:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:“long term” schools that are observed in the tax data at least 15 years. We see that about half of the revenue
22:733:72014:20:20:72:540:347:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:growth found for all schools in Table 3 is realized by these long term schools, with about half or slightly
22:734:72121:20:20:72:540:327:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:less of all enrollment growth accruing to these schools. Thus these schools, which make up about half of the
22:735:72229:20:20:72:540:307:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:sample, see roughly proportional-to-market-share revenue and enrollment increases. Subsidies thus increase
22:736:72335:20:20:72:540:287:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:enrollment and revenue from “long-standing” schools as well as relatively younger schools. Panel B shows
22:737:72439:20:20:72:365:267:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:that, as expected, these effects are stronger for restricted subsidies.
22:738:72510:20:20:86:539:247:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The last three columns in each panel run school-level regressions including school fixed effects so that
22:739:72614:20:19:72:540:227:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:these estimates will thus be identified from “intensive-margin” variation within schools over time. (Results
22:740:72722:19:20:72:540:208:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:without fixed effects, which would confound intensive-margin effects with other effects, are smaller and less
22:741:72831:20:20:72:540:188:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:precise). Column 4 in panel A suggests that revenue increases by about $59,000 for the typical school when
22:742:72937:20:20:72:540:168:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:a subsidy is introduced. There are about 110 schools in the typical state/year with a subsidy, so the revenue
22:743:73046:20:20:72:540:148:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:increase from changes within schools over time is about $6.5 million (59×110 = 6,490). This is notably
22:744:73148:20:20:72:540:128:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:smaller than the amount seen in the baseline estimates of even column 1 of Table 8–the revenue growth
22:745:73249:20:20:72:540:108:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f4:B:realized by long-term schools alone is larger than that suggested by within-school revenue growth forall
22:746:73353:20:0:72:540:88:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:schools. We return to this observation momentarily. Before doing so, we note that in column 5 of panel B
23:747:73459:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the revenue-per-student effects for unrestricted programs are smaller and less significant than before. This
23:748:73567:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:suggests that at least some of the driver for the increases in revenue-per-student is that students choose to
23:749:73676:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:attend more expensive schools than before (rather than schools raising revenue per student while enrollment
23:750:73783:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is static as a static-enrollment story would lead to larger estimates in panel B). Looking lastly at enrollment,
23:751:73895:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the story is much the same as with revenue, with relatively small within-school estimates. This suggests that
23:752:74004:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:extensive margin effects–changes in the number of schools–play an important role in changing revenue and
23:753:74108:20:20:72:165:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:enrollment outcomes.
23:754:74128:20:20:86:539:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 9 considers this by estimating regressions on the number of schools in a state/year observation. The
23:755:74234:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:first two columns, using all schools, suggest about 10 new schools for restricted programs and no growth for
23:756:74342:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:unrestricted. Table 2 indicates that the typical school in a subsidy state has about $2.5 million in revenue.
23:757:74451:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:If the marginal school driving the estimates in Table 9 has revenue close to the average, than revenue
23:758:74553:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:growth from the extensive margin would be about $25 million. Adding the $6.5 million intensive margin
23:759:74654:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:effect from the Table 8 estimates would produce revenue gains similar, and slightly larger, than the revenue
23:760:74762:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:effects estimated earlier (although the difference could be reconciled, for instance, if marginal schools had
23:761:74871:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:lower-than-average revenue). The estimates thus suggest that revenue increases both from extensive margin
23:762:74976:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:changes (changes in the number of schools) and intensive margin (within-school) revenue growth, with the
23:763:75080:20:20:72:202:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:former being quite important.
23:764:75109:20:20:86:539:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Interestingly, the next two columns of Table 9 suggest that this is in part driven by survival of long term
23:765:75216:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:schools (rather than just younger schools). The number of long-term schools increases with subsidies. Again
23:766:75323:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:using a $2.5 million average revenue-per-school, and supposing that the aggregate $6.5 million within-school
23:767:75431:20:20:72:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:estimate from Table 8 applies to this sample of schools, the implied revenue effect on long term schools would
23:768:75541:20:20:72:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:be 4×2.5 = $10 million on the extensive margin and $6.5 million on the intensive, for a total revenue effect
23:769:75649:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of about $16.5 million, which is close to the implied effect in the first column of Table 8 earlier. In other
23:770:75758:20:20:72:536:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:words, the discrepancy in revenue results in Table 8 can be resolved by appealing to the extensive margin.
23:771:75864:20:20:86:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Total school size can also be estimated for all schools in the PSS, not just those in the tax sample. The
23:772:75969:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:last two columns of Table 9 report results on the total number of schools each state/year in the PSS. As
23:773:76073:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:mentioned earlier our sample consists of about 1/9thof all schools, so that one might expect PSS coefficients
23:774:76182:20:19:71:539:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:about nine times as large. The coefficients suggest an effect about seven times as large, although again the
23:775:76290:19:20:71:539:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:precision of the estimates (which as before varies across inference methods) would allow the tax sample to
23:776:76396:20:20:71:539:132:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:be fully representative. In the next section, we use both the tax sample and the full PSS sample to make
23:777:76500:20:0:71:220:112:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:out-of-sample revenue predictions.
24:778:76536:0:27:72:274:710:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:C:5C. Effects for All Private Schools
24:779:76571:27:20:72:540:683:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:One could use these results to provide estimates for the effect of subsidies on all private school revenue.
24:780:76678:20:20:72:540:663:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Taking revenue as simplyP×Q, where the price is per-student revenue and quantity is total enrollment
24:781:76778:20:20:72:539:643:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(and is a function of price), for a small change in private school subsidization ratesubone would have
24:782:76880:20:20:72:540:623:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the change in revenue asδPδsubQ+PδQδPδPδsub. This formula might best be thought of as appropriate for small
24:783:76987:20:19:71:540:603:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:changes in a subsidy, but larger changes would require an additional “large effect” adjustment ofδPδsubδQδPδPδsub.
24:784:77101:19:34:71:212:584:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:This gives the revenue equation:
24:785:77133:34:13:231:242:550:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δP
24:786:77135:13:0:228:247:537:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δsub
24:787:77139:0:7:249:291:544:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:Q+PδQ
24:788:77144:7:0:278:289:537:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δP
24:789:77146:0:13:296:307:550:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δP
24:790:77148:13:0:293:312:537:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δsub
24:791:77152:0:7:316:341:544:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f9:A:+δP
24:792:77155:7:0:327:346:537:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δsub
24:793:77159:0:13:349:362:550:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δQ
24:794:77161:13:0:349:360:537:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δP
24:795:77163:0:13:367:378:550:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δP

24:797:77169:23:20:72:538:514:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f10:B:This is illustrated in figure 5; the two shaded areas represent the effect of a small change (where ∆Q=δQδPδPδsub
24:798:77282:20:20:72:540:494:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and ∆P=δPδsub), but the unshaded rectangle of area ∆P∆Qwill become relevant in calculations as the change
24:799:77387:20:20:71:187:474:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in the subsidy grows large.
24:800:77414:20:20:86:539:454:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Moreover, the basic supply/demand model in the figure obviously makes questionable assumptions for
24:801:77512:20:20:71:539:434:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the private education market; we note a few of these below but we can first check whether this revenue
24:802:77614:20:20:71:539:414:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:formula provides a decent within-sample estimate of the change in revenue from subsidization.  Further,
24:803:77717:20:20:71:539:394:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:since our estimates earlier provide the total change in revenue-per-student for all students, our use of the
24:804:77825:20:20:71:539:374:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:formula doesnotimpose the assumption in the picture that the subsidy raises prices for all students, nor
24:805:77929:20:20:71:391:354:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:does our calculation assume that all students are charged the same price.
24:806:78002:20:20:86:540:334:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Beginning with a within-sample use of the formula, we can estimate the incidence on schools,δPδsub, using
24:807:78107:20:19:72:538:314:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f10:B:column 1 of Table 6 divided by the per-student-funding estimate of $820, the enrollment changeδQδPδPδsub
24:808:78211:19:20:72:540:295:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:using column 1 of Table 7 over the per-student funding estimate, and forPandQwe can use the sample
24:809:78309:20:20:72:540:275:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:averages for states with subsidies, which are about $9,586 and 21,000 respectively (as opposed to using the
24:810:78416:20:50:72:388:255:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:full sample means in Table 2). This yields a calculated revenue effect of:
24:811:78490:50:14:102:113:205:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:C:δP

24:842:78585:39:20:72:308:162:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:which is quite close to the baseline estimate of 24,311.
24:843:78641:20:20:86:540:142:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Turning to estimates for all schools, we can estimateδQδPδPδsubusing the PSS estimate in column 5 of Table
24:844:78747:20:20:72:540:122:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:7 again divided by full-sample subsidy estimate of $820; we can use the PSS to calculate average enrollment
24:845:78854:20:20:72:540:102:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in our treatment states and we can estimate per-student revenue using average tuition in the Schools and
24:846:78958:20:0:72:540:82:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Staffing Survey (which is much smaller and available far fewer years than our sample, but will provide a
25:847:79064:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:representative national tuition average); the SASS data suggests that in 2003 (about the midpoint for our
25:848:79169:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:post-subsidy sample) that average tuition for all private schools in the country (in year 2012 dollars) was
25:849:79276:20:20:72:539:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:about $8250. Tuition in the SASS is based on highest charged tuition; it may overestimatePif tuition is
25:850:79379:20:20:71:539:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:lower for some students but it may underestimatePas it does not include other fees. We thus use $8250
25:851:79480:20:20:71:539:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:as a reasonable benchmark. The only term in the formula we do not have a direct estimate for is the price
25:852:79585:20:20:71:232:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:effect of subsidies for all schools,δP.
25:853:79624:20:20:86:539:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Table 10 reports estimated revenue effects, the estimated price elasticity of supply, and estimated dollar
25:854:79730:20:20:71:539:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for dollar effects for all private schools using the above revenue formula and a variety of assumptions for
25:855:79837:20:20:71:539:550:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f3:B:δP. We focus on the effects of all schools since the dollar-for-dollar effects appear to be roughly comparable
25:856:79947:20:20:71:539:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:across types of programs. The first row shows the change in revenue using the above formula (with block
25:857:80050:20:20:71:539:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:bootstrapped Conley-Taber-based 95% confidence intervals; their construction is described in the appendix),
25:858:80157:20:20:71:539:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the second row shows the assumed change in revenue per student, and the bottom two rows estimate the
25:859:80257:20:20:71:472:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:elasticity of supply and the effect on private school revenue for each dollar of funding spent.
25:860:80352:20:19:86:539:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:The first column reports estimates under the assumption that price (that is, revenue per student) does
25:861:80454:19:20:71:539:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:not change when subsidies are introduced; here any revenue increase comes from a quantity increase alone.
25:862:80559:20:20:71:539:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Column 2 assumes that the effect on per student revenue for all schools is half of the effect we estimate for
25:863:80668:20:20:71:539:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the tax sample. Column 3 assumes that the incidence of subsidies in our sample matches the incidence in
25:864:80771:20:20:71:539:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:all schools. Column 4 assumes that the incidence for other schools is twice the incidence in our sample, and
25:865:80879:20:20:71:283:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the last column assumes full incidence,δPδsub= 1.
25:866:80928:20:20:86:539:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Regardless of the assumption on how school subsidization affects revenue per student, we find that
25:867:81026:20:20:71:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsidies lead to large increases in revenue, withallestimates suggesting that funding raises revenue on
25:868:81130:20:20:71:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:close to a dollar-for-dollar basis or more. (Figure 5 shows how a greater-than-dollar-for-dollar effect is quite
25:869:81242:20:20:71:539:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:possible, as the rectangleABPDPScould easily be smaller than the area covered by the change in revenue.)
25:870:81346:20:20:71:539:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:A naive calculation of incidence could be made by simply multiplying our tax-sample revenue estimate 24,311
25:871:81453:20:20:71:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:by 5, implying a dollar for dollar effect of 5×24,311/57,420 = 2.12; which is comparable to several of the
25:872:81559:20:20:71:539:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:numbers in the table. Overall, Table 10 suggests that subsidies have large impacts on school revenue for
25:873:81663:20:20:71:539:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:all private schools, and that the results from our tax sample provide a reasonable first-order guide for all
25:874:81771:20:37:71:512:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:schools even if the incidence of schools out of our sample diverges widely from what we observe here.
25:875:81872:37:26:71:373:134:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:C:5D. Deadweight Loss and the Elasticity of Demand
25:876:81920:26:20:71:539:108:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:One could also use our estimates to calculate the deadweight loss of school subsidy programs. As a very simple
25:877:82030:20:0:71:539:88:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:starting point, one could simply estimate a traditional “deadweight loss triangle” created by the subsidy,
26:878:82138:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:where the base of the triangle would reflect the increase in student enrollment as a school subsidy program
26:879:82245:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is enacted, and the height of the triangle would be “per-student” spending on an average school subsidy
26:880:82348:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:program. We could then divide the area of the triangle by the total spent on the average subsidy program,
26:881:82453:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:creating a “deadweight-loss-per-dollar spent estimate.”  (The calculation of the triangle is illustrated in
26:882:82560:20:20:72:115:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Figure 5.)
26:883:82570:20:20:86:539:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Such a calculation of course involves several assumptions, including that the market structure would
26:884:82670:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:facilitate such a deadweight loss calculation and that our estimates can be extrapolated to private schools
26:885:82777:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:not in the sample. The latter assumption is implicitly made here when we calculate the height of the triangle
26:886:82886:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:as statewide subsidy spending over the statewide number of students assisted. (The revenue estimates in
26:887:82989:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Table 10 suggest that our results seem representative on some margins, and we can use our PSS enrollment
26:888:83093:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:estimates for this calculation.)  A more rigorous depiction of the market structure would have to take a
26:889:83197:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:strong stand on the objective function of private schools, which is beyond the scope of this paper. Here
26:890:83301:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:we take all the laws together for our calculation, although one could consider differing effects. Our simple
26:891:83409:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:calculation also ignores any change in deadweight loss from any “spillover” benefits from greater private
26:892:83514:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:education. The calculation also is static and ignores dynamic effects of the laws. Further, the calculation
26:893:83621:20:20:72:539:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:ignores any distortions in thepubliceducation sector and any changes to these distortions that subsidies
26:894:83725:20:20:71:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:create.38If subsidies lessen distortions stemming from the non-competitive public education sector, or
26:895:83827:20:20:71:539:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:improve educational outcomes generally in other ways, then the calculation here may be too pessimistic.
26:896:83930:20:20:71:539:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:These benefits of subsidies may also increase over time, so that dynamic considerations could again lead
26:897:84034:20:20:71:539:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:us to overstate deadweight loss here. On the other hand, if long-term enrollment gains from subsidies are
26:898:84139:20:20:71:539:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:larger than the estimates here (as households become more mobile over time), this will mechanically increase
26:899:84247:20:20:71:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:estimated deadweight loss (the triangle would get bigger), although even a much larger enrollment response
26:900:84353:20:20:71:539:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(e.g., twice as large as estimated here) would still lead to a relatively small deadweight loss calculation. Our
26:901:84465:20:20:71:539:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:estimate would also of course be larger by considering the distortionary effects of taxation (as taxes are raised
26:902:84578:20:20:71:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to pay for the subsidies), but, as noted below, the canonical estimates of this distortion are also relatively
26:903:84688:20:20:71:539:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:small. Given these caveats, we think presenting a deadweight loss estimate is worthwhile for motivating
26:904:84791:20:20:71:539:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:future work, for adding context to our estimates, and as a starting baseline, but we suggest the estimate be
26:905:84899:20:19:71:165:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:interpreted with care.
26:906:84921:19:20:86:539:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Using the PSS estimate which treats all laws the same in Table 7 to calculate deadweight loss in this way
26:907:85026:20:20:71:539:132:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:yields a deadweight loss per dollar spent of $0.03, with a 95% confidence interval (calculated using the delta
26:908:85136:20:16:71:539:112:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:method) of -0.04 to 0.11. We know of no similar calculation to put this into perspective, although we might
26:909:85243:16:12:79:540:96:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:38For a discussion of cross-market deadweight loss effects, as well as a discussion of deadweight loss calculation more generally,
26:910:85373:12:0:72:135:84:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:see Hines (1999).
27:911:85392:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:note that the canonical deadweight loss estimates of income taxation in Harberger (1964) for high-income
27:912:85496:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:families are similar. The fact that we estimate an (arguably) modest deadweight loss is not surprising, given
27:913:85605:20:20:72:301:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:that the estimate on student enrollment is not large.
27:914:85658:20:20:86:539:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:One could also use the coefficients here to construct an estimated elasticity of demand for private school.
27:915:85765:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Such an elasticity might best be interpreted as applying to marginal students responding to a subsidy
27:916:85866:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:program, and again will be based on the assumption that the relevant estimated price changes from our
27:917:85967:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:sample can extend to the population of private schools.  For this estimate we can take the increase in
27:918:86069:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:enrollment from the PSS estimate in Table 7 for the change in quantity. The change in price is generated from
27:919:86178:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the baseline per-student-revenue estimate in Table 6 minus average per-student subsidy funding for states
27:920:86283:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with a school subsidy law. Dividing the change in quantity by the change in price, and then multiplying by
27:921:86389:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the ratio of average price over average enrollment yields an elasticity of -0.52, with a 95% confidence interval
27:922:86501:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:of -1.1 to 0.07.39This is slightly larger in absolute value than the baseline elasticity of -0.19 in Dynarski,
27:923:86611:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Gruber, and Li (2009). However, as their elasticity might reflect the price responses of families with siblings
27:924:86722:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:already in private school, one might interpret our estimate as not surprising if families with siblings in private
27:925:86836:19:45:72:406:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:school are less price sensitive than marginal families responding to subsidies.
27:926:86915:45:30:72:149:386:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:C:Conclusion
27:927:86925:30:20:72:540:356:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:This paper investigates how private school subsidies impact private school finances.  Despite the current
27:928:87030:20:20:72:540:336:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:explosion of state-instituted subsidy programs, we know of no work that discusses the impacts of large
27:929:87132:20:20:72:540:316:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsidy programs on private schools, or that explores the incidence of school subsidies, or that calculates
27:930:87239:20:20:72:516:296:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:elasticities or traditional welfare effects in the context of large-scale school-choice-based subsidization.
27:931:87347:20:20:86:539:276:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:We find that school subsidy programs raise school revenues, and that the effects are dollar for dollar
27:932:87449:20:20:72:540:256:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:or larger.  There is evidence that different types of programs impact schools differently.  In particular,
27:933:87555:20:20:72:540:236:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:unrestricted and restricted subsidies appear to increase revenue in different ways, with the latter raising
27:934:87662:20:20:72:540:216:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:revenue through increases in student enrollment while the former raise revenue through increases in per-pupil
27:935:87771:20:20:72:540:196:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:expenditures.  As we note above, these results may suggest that schools view the market for students
27:936:87871:20:19:72:540:176:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:currently attending public schools as more competitive than is the market for students already enrolled in
27:937:87977:19:20:72:540:157:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:private school. Alternately, these results might suggest that private schools cream skim students, and that
27:938:88084:20:20:72:540:137:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the STOs and other organizations involved in providing restricted subsidies allow schools to cream skim
27:939:88187:20:15:72:540:117:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:students in a way that other subsidy programs do not. Back-of-the-envelope calculations indicate that the
27:940:88292:15:12:79:540:102:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:39The elasticity estimate uses coefficients from two different regressions; we estimate them together using Seemingly Unrelated
27:941:88419:12:10:72:540:90:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:Regression and use the resulting variance-covariance matrix for estimating the variance of the elasticity when applying the delta
27:942:88548:10:0:72:102:80:8:g_d0_f7:8:g_d0_f7:F:method.
28:943:88557:0:20:72:417:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:deadweight loss of school subsidies overall may be reasonably moderate in size.
28:944:88636:20:20:86:539:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:There are several caveats to our findings. First, as noted above, even in cases where our results suggest
28:945:88741:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f4:B:no change in enrollment in the face of school subsidization, this does not necessarily mean that thecompo-
28:946:88847:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f3:B:sitionof students stayed the same after subsidies were introduced. Even if total enrollment is unchanged it
28:947:88954:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:could be that school subsidies allow newly marginal students to “crowd out” other students who are either
28:948:89059:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:screened out, or unwilling to pay the higher tuition (or both) following the policy reform. For example,
28:949:89163:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:subsidization may have led nontraditional (e.g., minority) students towards private education. Rinz (2015)
28:950:89269:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:gives a rigorous investigation of this possibility, and considers how large voucher programs may have changed
28:951:89378:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:student/teacher ratios and other educational inputs for students of different races. Next, while our laws have
28:952:89488:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:commonalities with many very-recently-enacted laws, the overall impact of these new laws will only be known
28:953:89595:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:with certainty in the future. Additionally, the simulation-based work noted in the introduction often assumes
28:954:89704:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:fully mobile households; if household mobility is greater over a long period of time, then the findings here
28:955:89812:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:may underestimate the long-term impacts of subsidies. Extending our results to more recent programs as
28:956:89914:20:0:72:369:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:they grow in the future represents an excellent area for future work.

33:1069:98560:30:20:72:106:605:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:C:Arizona
33:1070:98567:20:20:86:539:585:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:In 1997, Arizona offered a tax credit to individual taxpayers for donations made to STOs. The credit was
33:1071:98671:20:20:72:540:565:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:initially equal to the amount of the donation made and capped at $500. STOs are required to spend at least
33:1072:98777:20:20:72:540:545:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:90% of the money they receive on scholarships and may not limit scholarship recipients to only one school.
33:1073:98883:20:20:72:540:525:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Some details of Arizona’s credit have changed over time. Married and unmarried taxpayers were initially
33:1074:98986:20:20:72:540:505:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:eligible for the same $500 credit, but over time, the limit for married taxpayers has gradually increased to
33:1075:99094:20:20:72:540:485:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:$1,000. Since the program’s inception, donors have been prohibited from requiring that their gifts be used
33:1076:99200:20:19:72:540:465:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to fund a scholarship for any of their dependents. However, there is evidence that in fact many parents did
33:1077:99307:19:20:72:540:446:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:ensure that their contributions resulted in scholarship support for their children, either by “suggesting” their
33:1078:99419:20:20:72:540:426:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:child as a recipient or by (e.g.) making a quid-pro-quo agreement with another family where each family
33:1079:99522:20:20:72:540:406:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:would designate a gift towards the other family’s child (Reese, 2009; Bland, 2000).  In response to such
33:1080:99626:20:20:72:540:386:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:behavior Arizona later (after the period of this study) prohibited donors from designating their gifts for any
33:1081:99736:20:20:72:153:366:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:particular student.
33:1082:99755:20:20:86:539:346:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Through 2009, Arizona’s individual tax credit was claimed by an average of just over 55,000 taxpayers
33:1083:99856:20:20:72:540:326:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:per year, with each donating an average of $700 to STOs (2012 dollars).  Over the same period, STOs
33:1084:99955:20:20:72:540:306:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:awarded an average of 19,000 scholarships annually, with the average scholarship being worth nearly $1,700
33:1085:100061:20:20:72:539:286:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(2012 dollars).40Neither the scholarships nor the tax credits that generate the funding that supports them
33:1086:100167:20:20:72:540:266:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are means tested, and individual scholarship award amounts are not limited by law. Some students may
33:1087:100267:20:20:72:540:246:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:receive scholarships from multiple STOs. STOs may use any criteria in awarding scholarships, but must
33:1088:100368:20:20:72:540:226:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:consider financial need. Details of that consideration are not specified, however, and this seems to amount
33:1089:100475:20:20:72:540:206:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to a requirement to collect information on income from scholarship applicants. Scholarship applicants face
33:1090:100581:20:20:72:540:186:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:no eligibility requirements, and schools receiving scholarship students are required only to abide by a limited
33:1091:100692:20:19:72:540:166:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:set of non-discrimination rules. Schools with scholarship students are not prohibited from discriminating on
33:1092:100800:19:20:72:160:147:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the basis of religion.
33:1093:100822:20:20:86:539:127:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Arizona’s corporate tax credit, which was launched in 2006, is somewhat more restrictive on certain
33:1094:100921:20:15:72:540:107:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:dimensions than its individual credit. Although there is no per taxpayer limit on the credit, there is an
33:1095:101026:15:0:79:328:92:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:40Minnesota House of Representatives Research Department, 2011.
34:1096:101091:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:aggregate limit on the total value of credits awarded each year. In return for donations to STOs, tax credits
34:1097:101200:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are awarded dollar-for-dollar to corporate taxpayers on a first-come, first-served basis until the aggregate
34:1098:101308:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:limit is reach. Over our period of study, the aggregate limit ranged from $10 million to $17 million annually
34:1099:101417:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:(though the cap was not reached each year). Taxpayers may not designate their contributions for use by
34:1100:101519:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:particular students.  STOs must spend at least 90% of revenue they receive through the corporate tax
34:1101:101619:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:credit on scholarships for children with family incomes below 185% of the federal free or reduced price lunch
34:1102:101728:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:program cutoff. In order to receive a scholarship, students must have been enrolled in a public school the
34:1103:101834:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:previous year, be just starting school, or have previously received a scholarship. Scholarships were capped
34:1104:101941:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:at $4,200 - $4,500 for grades K-8 and $5,500 - $5,800 for grades 9-12 during our period of study. Very few
34:1105:102047:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:scholarships were awarded in the program’s first year, but from 2007-2009, Arizona’s corporate tax credit
34:1106:102152:20:20:72:484:510:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f5:B:financed over 2,800 scholarships per year worth an average of $2,500 annually (2012 dollars).41
34:1107:102247:20:20:72:104:490:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:C:Florida
34:1108:102254:20:20:86:539:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:In 2001, Florida established a tax credit for corporate tax payers that made donations to organizations
34:1109:102357:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:similar to Arizona’s STOs. The credit is limited to 75 percent of a taxpayer’s liability, although taxpayers
34:1110:102465:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:can carry forward unused claims for up to three years. Credits are awarded dollar for dollar with donations,
34:1111:102573:20:20:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:excluding the amount by which donations may affect federal tax liability. As Table 1 shows, scholarships
34:1112:102677:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:awarded by STOs under this program are restricted to low income students. The maximum scholarship
34:1113:102774:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:awarded ranged from $3,500 in 2002 up to $3,950 in 2009. Over that period, donations funded scholarships
34:1114:102878:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for 18,000 students per year on average. STOs award new scholarships on a first-come, first-served basis;
34:1115:102983:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:priority is given to scholarships being renewed. STOs may not reserve scholarships for use at particular
34:1116:103087:20:20:72:508:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:private schools or award scholarships to the children of owner/operators of STOs or private schools.
34:1117:103187:20:20:86:539:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:To be eligible to receive a scholarship, students must be eligible for free or reduced-price school lunches
34:1118:103294:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:and have been enrolled in a public school the year before, have received a scholarship from an STO the year
34:1119:103401:20:20:72:540:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:before, or be entering kindergarten through fifth grade. Siblings living with students meeting these criteria
34:1120:103510:20:20:72:540:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are also eligible for scholarships, as are students currently or recently placed in foster care. Scholarships may
34:1121:103623:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:be renewed as long as the student’s family income remains below 230% of the federal poverty level (below
34:1122:103727:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:200% FPL before 2008). Between 200% and 230% FPL, the value of the scholarship is reduced. Students
34:1123:103826:20:19:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:may not receive multiple scholarships. STOs may also award scholarships to cover the cost of transportation
34:1124:103933:19:20:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to a public school outside a student’s home district, but the share of students receiving this type of support
34:1125:104043:20:20:72:540:132:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:is small (Hoxby and Murarka, 2007). Taxpayers may not designate specific children to benefit from their
34:1126:104146:20:20:72:172:112:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:contributions to STOs.
34:1127:104168:20:15:86:539:92:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Florida’s corporate tax credit program was preceded by the establishment of the McKay Voucher program
34:1128:104269:15:0:79:328:77:6:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:F:41Minnesota House of Representatives Research Department, 2011.
35:1129:104334:0:20:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in 1999. This voucher program provides fairly generous voucher payments to students with disabilities (as
35:1130:104439:20:20:72:540:690:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:defined in Table 1). The maximum possible payment equals the lesser of (a) the amount the student would
35:1131:104542:20:20:72:540:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:have “received” in public school or (b) the amount of the private school’s tuition and fees. In order to be
35:1132:104649:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:eligible for a voucher, students must have spent the previous year in a public school or the Florida School
35:1133:104756:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for the Deaf and Blind, or have received specialized instruction under a current individualized education
35:1134:104861:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:plan, or be the dependent child of a member of the military who was recently relocated to Florida. Students
35:1135:104968:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:participating in the program may attend another public school that meets their needs or receive a voucher
35:1136:105073:20:20:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:for use at a participating private school. Vouchers are not means tested and can be used until the student
35:1137:105179:20:20:72:540:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:returns to public school, graduates, or turns 22 years old. Although Table 1 shows that this program began
35:1138:105285:20:20:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in 1999, participation in the program was quite limited in its first two years (the program in 1999 was in
35:1139:105391:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:fact a pilot program). From 2001 through 2009, vouchers were awarded to an average of 15,600 students
35:1140:105492:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:per year, with per student spending averaging nearly $7,300 (2012 dollars) over that period.42Although
35:1141:105594:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:students in the program have the option of attending a different public school, these figures pertain only to
35:1142:105703:20:19:72:214:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:vouchers used in private schools.
35:1143:105736:19:20:72:93:431:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:C:Iowa
35:1144:105740:20:20:86:539:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Iowa’s credit for tuition and textbook expenses was enacted in 1987; it initially offered a non-refundable,
35:1145:105847:20:20:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:5% credit on the first $1,000 of tuition and textbook expenses per dependent to tax filers with adjusted
35:1146:105951:20:20:72:540:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:gross income under $45,000 who claimed the standard deduction or a deduction of up to $1,000 to those
35:1147:106052:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:who itemized. In 1996, the deduction was eliminated, the tax credit was made available to all filers, the
35:1148:106157:20:20:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:match rate was increased to 10%, and the income restriction was eliminated. In 1998, the match rate was
35:1149:106260:20:20:72:540:311:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:again increased to 25%, and the list of eligible expenditures was expanded to include some fees, as well as
35:1150:106367:20:20:72:540:291:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:expenses associated with extracurricular activities. Expenses related to the teaching of religious doctrines
35:1151:106475:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:are not eligible; at religiously affiliated schools, credits are pro-rated based on the share of time students
35:1152:106585:20:20:72:202:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:spend in non-religious classes.
35:1153:106616:20:20:86:539:231:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:From 1998-2009, the portion of our analysis period in which Iowa’s tax credit was in widespread use,
35:1154:106716:20:20:72:540:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:about 162,000 Iowa taxpayers claimed it annually, receiving an average credit of $99 (2012 dollars).43Iowa’s
35:1155:106824:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:credit is applicable to expenses incurred on behalf of dependent students at accredited public and private
35:1156:106930:20:19:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:schools in Iowa.  In 2010, 7.2 percent of claimants reported the maximum allowable level of educational
35:1157:107033:19:20:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:expenses (i.e. $1,000 per dependent child), claiming the maximum credit. Iowa does not collect information

36:1163:107573:20:20:72:102:690:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:C:Illinois
36:1164:107581:20:20:86:539:670:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:In 1999, Illinois adopted a credit providing a non-refundable credit equal to 25% of educational expenses
36:1165:107686:20:20:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:above $250 incurred on behalf of dependent children. The maximum value of the credit is $500 per family.
36:1166:107790:20:20:72:540:630:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Eligible expenses include private school tuition as well as book and lab fees that may be incurred by students
36:1167:107900:20:20:72:540:610:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in either public or private schools. Observers have noted that while the program can cover public-school
36:1168:108004:20:20:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:expenses, families with public-school students “receive very little in the form of tax credits...the credit serves

36:1170:108229:20:20:72:123:550:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:Way, 2003).
36:1171:108240:20:20:86:539:530:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:In order for a student’s expenses to be eligible for this credit, that student must be an Illinois resident,
36:1172:108348:20:20:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:under 21 years of age at the end of the school year for which the credit is claimed, and enrolled full time
36:1173:108455:20:20:72:540:490:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:in one a K-12 program that provides instruction in English, including homeschooling. Schools must be in
36:1174:108558:20:20:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:compliance with Title IV of the Civil Rights Act of 1964 in order for their students expenses to qualify for
36:1175:108666:20:19:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:the tax credit. Between 2000 and 2009, an average of 214,000 families claimed the tax credit each year. The
36:1176:108773:19:20:72:540:431:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:average tax credit over that period was worth $382 (2012 dollars).45This level of tax credit implies average
36:1177:108881:20:20:72:319:411:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:educational expenses of more than $1,775 (2012 dollars).
36:1178:108937:20:20:72:129:391:10:g_d0_f4:10:g_d0_f4:C:Pennsylvania
36:1179:108949:20:20:86:539:371:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:A:Pennsylvania enacted a corporate credit in 2001; corporations can claim a nonrefundable credit for
36:1180:109047:20:20:72:540:351:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:contributions to STOs (although the state’s legislation refers to them simply as “scholarship organizations”)

36:1184:109471:20:20:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:million in 2009. A tax credit for any corporation is limited to $300,000 per tax year. Tax credits are not
36:1185:109577:20:20:72:407:251:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:awarded for activities that are part of the normal course of a firm’s business.

36:1188:109863:20:20:72:540:191:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:to less restrictive family income requirements. Scholarships must be used within Pennsylvania at schools
36:1189:109967:20:19:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:that comply with Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964. Between the 2001 and 2009 academic years,

36:1191:110173:20:20:72:540:132:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:B:funding scholarships averaging $1,260 for 31,500 students annually (2012 dollars).46Beginning in 2004,
